His statement: (and the servant if he is wealthy) meaning the maintenance and clothing are obligatory for the servant of the woman because her sufficiency is obligatory upon him and this is part of its completion since she cannot do without him. So he is obliged to give the servant the minimum sufficiency not reaching the woman's maintenance, and similarly his clothing at the cheapest possible, and a light shoe is prescribed for the servant because she needs to go out unlike the woman; thus in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Hidaya, the maintenance of the servant is interpreted as what is required for the indigent of his wife's maintenance. And in al-Bada'i' and Sharh al-Tahawi, a condition for the obligation of her servant's maintenance is that he has no occupation other than serving her, i.e., he is free for her. The author used the term 'servant' absolutely and did not attribute it to her due to difference in its interpretation: some say it is anyone who serves her, free or slave, owned by her or by him or by both or by others. And the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) from our three masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) as in al-Dhakhira is that it is her slave; so if she has no servant, the husband is not required to provide for a servant because it is due to her ownership of it. So if on the apparent narration of stipulating the servant be owned by her, then if it is not owned by her, there is no maintenance for it on the husband even if she is in need of it, as understood from the author's statement 'and the author used 'servant' absolutely' etc. Consider his statement: (and the apparent narration from our three masters) etc. The wording of al-Dhakhira is: he said, 'If the woman has no servant, the maintenance of the servant is not prescribed for the husband in the apparent narration from our three masters, because her entitlement to the servant's maintenance is by virtue of owning the servant; if she has no servant, how can she claim the servant's maintenance? It is analogous to the judge, etc.' I say: This wording is not explicit stipulating that the servant be owned by her. His statement: (there is consideration) Al-Ramli said: if he said 'there is deficiency'... If it is not in her ownership, her maintenance is not required, like the judge if he has no servant he does not deserve a servant's maintenance from the treasury. And the apparent meaning of their speech is that her servant is the one owned by her, whether it is a male or female slave. For this reason it is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that 'the servant' is one, and 'servants' are male or female. And by this it becomes clear that al-Zayla'i's interpretation of 'her servant' as the female slave owned by her in the apparent narration is questionable, and it should include the mudabbar and mudabbara. And by this it is known that if she has no owned servant, the husband is not required to hire a boy to serve her, but he must buy for her what she needs from the market as stated in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya. And he restricted it to 'servant' because he is not obliged to provide for more than one servant for her, and this is according to him (Abu Hanifa) and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: He is required to provide for two servants because she needs one for indoor affairs and the other for outdoor affairs. Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) argument is that one can handle both matters, so there is no need for two. Al-Tahawi said: The author of al-Imla' narrated from Abu Yusuf that if the woman is of such high status that one servant is not enough for her, then he spends on as many servants as she cannot do without, whether more than one or two or more. He said: And we take this; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in al-Zahiriya and al-Walwalijiya: If the woman is from the daughters of nobles and has servants, the husband is compelled to provide for two servants. So the conclusion is that the school (madhhab) is to limit to one absolutely, but the adopted view among the later scholars (mashayikh) is the statement of Abu Yusuf. In Fath al-Qadir and al-Dhakhira: If he has children and one servant is insufficient for them, then two or more are prescribed according to what suffices them, by agreement. In al-Tajnis: A woman who has slaves said to her husband: 'Spend on them from my mahr,' and he spent, then she said: 'I do not make it from the mahr because you employed them.' Whatever he spent in a reasonable manner is counted against her because it was by her command. And he (the author) used absolute terms in the obligation of the servant's maintenance, so it includes whether the husband wants to serve her himself or have his female servant serve her without spending on her servant. It is stated in al-Khaniyya: If the husband says, 'I will serve you or a slave-girl of mine will serve you,' the correct view is that the husband does not have the right to expel the woman's servant from her house. Al-Walwaliji explained it by saying that perhaps the wife may not be ready to be served by the husband's service. Its apparent meaning is that he has the right to expel more than one servant from her house because it is beyond what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said. And he (the author) used absolute terms for 'woman' so it includes the slave woman, the free noblewoman, and the lowly. But in al-Khulasa attributed to al-Fatawa al-Sughra: A married woman who is a slave does not deserve the servant's maintenance; the servant's maintenance is for the daughters of nobles. And it is inconceivable that a slave woman has a servant according to the apparent narration because it is owned by the woman and a slave has no ownership. This is only according to the one who interpreted 'servant' as any servant, owned or not. Some have taken what is in al-Khulasa that if she is from the lowly class, she does not deserve the servant's maintenance. It would have been better if he had said... but it is responded that it is based on the usual practice of women taking servants from the category of slave girls, not that it is a restriction. Consider his statement: (and Abu Yusuf said: He prescribes two servants) etc. Al-Ramli said: I say: and from Abu Yusuf in another narration, meaning other than the narration of two servants, that if the woman is superior, daughter of a superior, and is brought to her husband's house with many servants, she deserves the maintenance of all the servants from the husband. If the husband says to his wife, 'I will not spend on anyone of...'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the obligation of a husband to provide maintenance (nafaqa) and clothing (kiswa) for his wife's servant. The author begins by explaining the general ruling: if the husband is wealthy, he must provide for the servant, but at the minimum level—less than the wife's own maintenance, and with the cheapest clothing and light shoes, since the servant goes out unlike the wife. The condition from some sources (Bada'i', Sharh al-Tahawi) is that the servant must be exclusively dedicated to serving the wife, with no other occupation. The author notes that the term 'servant' (khadim) is ambiguous: it could be any person serving her, whether free or slave, owned by her, the husband, or others. However, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) from the three eponyms (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani), the servant must be the wife's property (mamluk). If she does not own a servant, the husband is not required to provide for one. The analogy is given with a judge who does not receive a servant from the treasury if he does not have one. The commentator then critiques al-Zayla'i's interpretation restricting 'servant' to a female slave, arguing that it should include mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death) as well. He also clarifies that if the wife has no owned servant, the husband is not obliged to hire a servant but must buy necessary items for her from the market. The core dispute emerges regarding the number of servants: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that only one servant is obligatory, as one can handle both indoor and outdoor tasks. Abu Yusuf, however, argues for two—one for inside and one for outside—and in a different narration, even more if the wife's status requires it. Later scholars (mashayikh) adopted Abu Yusuf's view. The passage also discusses whether the husband can serve the wife himself instead of providing a servant: the correct position is that he cannot expel the wife's own servant from her house, and she is not obliged to accept his personal service. Regarding the type of woman, a slave wife does not qualify for a servant under the apparent narration since she cannot own property. The commentary concludes with Abu Yusuf's alternative narration that a high-status wife who enters with many servants merits maintenance for all of them. The text is rich with intra-madhhab reasoning and practical applications.

Key Terms
خادم (khadim): The servant of the woman; in this context, primarily a female slave (jariya) owned by the wife, but extended to any person serving her, free or slave, owned by her, husband, or others.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known transmitted opinions from the three Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) as compiled in the six canonical works of Muhammad al-Shaybani.
نفقة الخادم (nafaqat al-khadim): Maintenance and clothing for the wife's servant, obligatory on the husband if he is wealthy and the servant is owned by her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If the servant is a free person hired by the husband.
B) If the servant is owned by the wife (mamluk laha).
C) If the servant is a male slave only.
D) If the wife requests any servant regardless of ownership.

Key Takeaway
The husband's obligation to provide for his wife's servant is conditional on the servant being owned by her and dedicated to her service, and the later Hanafi position allows for multiple servants based on need.

Mukatab's Child Maintenance: Liability and Exceptions
States ruling on mukatab's child maintenance with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on child maintenance for a mukatab and identify the scenarios where liability shifts to the mother or to the mukatab himself.

Core Idea
A mukatab is not liable for child maintenance unless the child is from his own slave-girl.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a nuanced area of Hanafi family law: the maintenance obligations of a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) for his children. The general rule is that the mukatab is not responsible, but the reasoning reveals important principles about status and dependency. Understanding these exceptions helps apply the rule accurately in different domestic arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the general ruling
The subject is a mukatab's child maintenance. The ruling states that it is not obligatory (wajib) on the mukatab, regardless of whether the wife is free or a full slave.
The ruling applies to the mukatab's obligation, not the mother's.
2
Understand the talil (reasoning) for the mother's liability
When both parents are mukatab belonging to the same master, the child follows the mother's kitaba. Evidence: the child's earnings, blood money (arsh al-jinaya), and inheritance go to the mother. Therefore, maintenance is also on her.
This is based on the principle that the child's legal status and financial matters follow the mother's kitaba.
3
Contrast the exception
If the mukatab fathers a child with his own female slave (who is not a mukataba), then the child follows the father's kitaba. Hence, maintenance becomes wajib on the mukatab, similar to his own maintenance.
The child is considered 'a part of him' and included in his kitaba.
4
Extract the underlying legal principle
The ruling revolves around attachment to kitaba: whoever the child's legal status (earnings, blood money, inheritance) follows, that person is responsible for maintenance. This is consistent across the scenarios.
This is a key Hanafi principle for determining obligations in partial slavery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الولوالجية زاد في الكافي للحاكم وشرحه للسرخسي وشرح الطحاوي والشامل: وكذلك المكاتب لا تجب نفقة ولده سواء كانت امرأته حرة أو قنة لهذا المعنى. وإذا كانت امرأة المكاتب مكاتبة وهما لمولى واحد فنفقة الولد على الأم لأن الولد تابع للأم في كتابتها، ولهذا كان كسب الولد لها وأرش الجناية عليه لها وميراثه لها فكذلك النفقة تكون عليها بخلاف ما إذا وطىء المكاتب أمته فولدت حيث تجب نفقة الولد على المكاتب لأنه داخل في كتابته ولهذا يكون كسبه له، وكذا أرش الجناية عليه له ولأنه جزؤه فإذا تبعه في العقد كانت نفقته عليه كنفقة نفسه اهـ.
Al-Walwaliji added in al-Kafi by al-Hakim and its commentary by al-Sarakhsi and Sharh al-Tahawi and al-Shamil: Similarly, the mukatab's child maintenance is not obligatory, whether his wife is free or a slave for this meaning. If the wife of the mukatab is also a mukataba and both belong to one master, then the maintenance of the child is upon the mother because the child follows the mother in her kitaba, thus the child's earnings, blood money, and inheritance are for her, so similarly maintenance is upon her, unlike if the mukatab had intercourse with his slave-girl and she gave birth, where the maintenance of the child is upon the mukatab because he is included in his kitaba, thus his earnings are for him, and likewise the blood money is for him, and because the child is a part of him, so if he follows him in the contract, his maintenance is upon him like his own maintenance. End.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the maintenance of a child born to a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission). The ruling is that the mukatab is not obligated to maintain his child, whether the wife is free or a slave. The reason is that the child follows the mother in her status. If both parents are mukatab belonging to the same master, the mother is responsible for the child's maintenance because the child is attached to her kitaba, as evidenced by the child's earnings, blood money, and inheritance going to the mother. An exception is made if the mukatab fathers a child with his own slave-girl: then the maintenance is on the mukatab because the child is part of his kitaba and follows him, just like his own maintenance.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum for freedom.
النفقة (al-nafaqa): Financial maintenance including food, clothing, and shelter owed to dependents.
القنة (al-qinn): A full slave (male or female) who is not a mukatab or mudabbar.
الكتابة (al-kitaba): The contract of manumission between a slave and master, specifying a payment schedule for freedom.
أرش الجناية (arsh al-jinaya): Blood money or compensation paid for injury or homicide, which goes to the victim's guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the basis for the child's maintenance being upon the mother when both parents are mukatab?
A) The child follows the father's lineage.
B) The child follows the mother's kitaba.
C) The master is responsible.
D) The child is considered independent.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's child maintenance is not on him unless the child is born from his own slave-girl, because maintenance follows attachment to kitaba.

Selling the Slave for Overdue Alimony
Ruling on selling a slave for alimony arrears

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a slave can be sold for overdue alimony according to the Hanafi school, and identify the master's option to redeem.

Core Idea
A slave husband is not sold for trivial alimony; sale requires accumulation beyond his ability to pay, unless the master redeems.

In Hanafi law, a slave husband is responsible for his wife's alimony from his own earnings. If he fails to pay and the master does not intercede, the question arises: can the slave be sold? This lesson from al-Dhakhira provides the threshold: only when alimony accumulates to an amount the slave cannot afford.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize the parties: a slave husband, a wife entitled to alimony, and the master. The judge has fixed monthly alimony.
2
Determine the condition for sale
The sale is not immediate for any overdue amount. It requires that the alimony arrears accumulate to a sum the slave cannot pay. This avoids harm to the master from selling for a trivial amount.
The text: 'فإذا اجتمع عليه من النفقة ما يعجز عن الأداء يباع فيه'.
3
Note the master's right to redeem
The master can prevent the sale by paying the overdue alimony (fidaa). If he redeems, the slave is not sold.
The exception: 'إلا أن يفديه المولى'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم أر متى يباع القن في النفقة فإن القاضي إذا قرر لها نفقة كل شهر كذا وطالبت بالنفقة هل يباع لأجل النفقة اليسيرة أو تصبر المرأة حتى يجتمع لها من قيمته؟ إن قلنا بالأول ففيه إضرار بالمولى ويقتضي أن يباع في نفقة يوم إذا طلبتها ولم يفده السيد، وإن قلنا بالثاني ففيه إضرار بها خصوصاً إذا كانت فقيرة. وذكر في الذخيرة ما يدل على المراد ولفظها : فإذا اجتمع عليه من النفقة ما يعجز عن الأداء يباع فيه إلا أن يفديه المولى اهـ. فإذا فرض القاضي لها نفقة شهر مثلاً فطالبته وعجز عن أدائه باعه القاضي إن لم يفده والله الموفق للصواب
I have not seen when the qinn (owned slave) is sold for alimony. If the judge fixes for her alimony each month, and she demands the alimony, is he sold for a small amount of alimony or does the woman wait until she accumulates from its value? If we say the first, there is harm to the master and it would require selling him for one day's alimony if she demands it and the master does not pay. If we say the second, there is harm to her, especially if she is poor. Mentioned in al-Dhakhira: When the alimony accumulates to an amount he cannot pay, he is sold for it unless the master redeems him. End. So if the judge fixes for her alimony for a month, and she demands it and he is unable to pay, the judge sells him if the master does not redeem him. And Allah is the guide to correctness.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage raises the practical question of when a slave husband can be sold to pay overdue alimony. The author notes a dilemma: selling for a small amount harms the master, but delaying harms the poor wife. The solution from al-Dhakhira is that the slave is sold only when the alimony accumulates to an amount he cannot pay, unless the master redeems him. The judge can sell the slave for one month's alimony if the master refuses to pay.

Key Terms
القن (al-qinn): An owned slave (not a mukatab or mudabbar).
يفديه (yafdihi): The master redeems the slave by paying the alimony debt to prevent his sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the alimony accumulates to an amount he cannot pay

D) Never

Key Takeaway
A slave may be sold for alimony only when arrears accumulate beyond his capacity to pay, unless the master redeems.

Multiple Sales of a Slave for Alimony vs. Mahr
Clarifies that a slave can be sold multiple times for new alimony, unlike mahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits multiple sales of a slave for alimony but not for mahr, and apply the distinction between a recurring debt and a one-time obligation.

Core Idea
Alimony is incremental, each arrear a new debt; mahr is a single obligation, so slave sold only once.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a slave owned by a debtor can be sold to satisfy alimony (nafaqah) owed to his wife. The key ruling is that such a sale can be repeated for each new alimony arrear because alimony arises gradually, while mahr (dower) is a fixed debt for which the slave can be sold only once. The text also discusses the buyer's right of return if unaware of the debt. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how Hanafi jurists treat recurring vs. one-time obligations in the context of property sales for debt satisfaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the Hanafi position: a slave can be sold multiple times for alimony, but only once for mahr. Note that the ruling applies to any master of the slave, not only the one who married him to the wife.
The ruling is explicitly for alimony debt; for mahr, the slave is sold once for the entire amount.
2
Isolate the effective cause (illah)
The text states that alimony 'is renewed little by little' (تجب شيئاً فشيئاً), meaning each new arrear is a separate debt. Mahr, on the other hand, is a single, undivided obligation. This incremental nature is the illah for permitting repeated sales.
The illah is the gradual accrual of alimony over time.
3
Analyze the buyer's position
If the buyer knows about the alimony debt at purchase or later consents, the sale is valid and the slave can be sold again for new alimony. If the buyer was unaware and did not consent, he may return the slave as defective.
This shows that the ruling balances creditor rights and buyer protection.
4
Confirm the distinction with mahr
For mahr, the entire debt is obligatory from the start. If the slave is sold for mahr, the sale extinguishes the entire debt for that marriage. No new mahr debt arises, so no second sale.
The text explicitly contrasts: 'the slave is sold for all of the dower because the entire dower is obligatory'.
5
Apply the principle to a modern analogy
Consider a court order for monthly child support. Each month's payment is a separate debt upon accrual. If a debtor's asset is sold to cover one month, the same asset can be sold again for the next month's arrear. In contrast, a one-time obligation like a loan principal can only be enforced by sale once.
This is a forward mapping: the classical illah applies to modern recurring obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في بيعه لها فشمل سيده المزوج له وغيره، فإذا بيع فيها فاشتراه من علم به أو لم يعلم ثم علم فرضي ظهر السبب في حقه أيضاً، فإذا اجتمعت عليه النفقة مرة أخرى يباع ثانياً، وكذا حاله عند المشتري الثالث وهلم جراً. ولا يباع مرة بعد أخرى إلا في دين النفقة لأنها تتجدد شيئاً فشيئاً على حسب تجدد الزمان على وجه يظهر في حق السيد فهو في الحقيقة دين حادث عند المشتري، وأما إذا لم يعلم المشتري بحاله أو علم بعد الشراء ولم يرض فله رده لأن عيب اطلع عليه ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد فرق الولوالجي وغيره أيضاً النفقة وبين دين المهر بأن العبد إنما بيع في جميع المهر فإن المهر جميعه واجب، فإذا بين دين بيع في جميع المهر مرة لا يباع مرة أخرى وإن بقي شيء من ذلك المهر، فأما النفقة فإنما تجب شيئاً فشيئاً فإذا بيع فيها فإنما بيع فيما اجتمع من النفقة وصارت واجبة، وأما فيما لم يجتمع ولم يصر واجباً لا يتصور البيع فيه، فإذا وجبت نفقة أخرى فهذا دين حادث لم يبع العبد فيه مرة أخرى فجاز بيعه اهـ.
And he generalized in his sale for her, so it includes her master who married her to him and others. So if he is sold for it and a person buys him who knows about it or does not know, then he knows and is satisfied, the cause becomes apparent also for him. So if alimony accumulates on him again, he is sold a second time, and similarly his case with the third buyer, and so on. He is not sold again after that except for alimony debt because it is renewed little by little according to the renewal of time in a way that appears for the master, so in reality it is a new debt for the buyer. But if the buyer does not know his situation, or knows after purchase and is not satisfied, he may return him because it is a defect he discovered. This is from Fath al-Qadir. And al-Walwaliji and others also distinguished between alimony and the debt of dower: the slave is sold for all of the dower because the entire dower is obligatory, so if he is sold for the dower debt once, he is not sold again even if something remains of that dower. As for alimony, it becomes obligatory little by little, so if he is sold for it, he is sold only for the alimony that has accumulated and become obligatory. As for what has not accumulated and not become obligatory, sale for it is not conceivable. So if another alimony becomes obligatory, this is a new debt for which the slave has not been sold before, so his sale is permissible. End.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage clarifies two points: first, a slave sold for alimony can be sold again for new alimony that accrues after the first sale, because alimony is a recurring debt. The buyer, if aware of the debt at the time of purchase, cannot return the slave unless he was unaware. Second, alimony differs from dower (mahr), which is a single obligation where the slave is sold once for the entire amount and cannot be sold again for the same debt. Alimony, being incremental, allows for repeated sales as new debts arise.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The bridal gift (dower) owed by the husband to the wife, due at marriage.
دين حادث (dayn hadith): A new debt that arises after the previous debt was settled.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Alimony or maintenance owed to a wife or dependent, which accrues over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Alimony is more important.
B) Alimony is a recurring debt arising gradually.
C) Dower is forgiven easily.
D) The master prefers it.

Key Takeaway
The incremental nature of alimony makes each new arrear a separate debt, permitting repeated sales of the same slave, unlike the one-time mahr obligation.

Critique of Sadr al-Shari'a's Ruling on Slave Sale for Alimony
Corrects a previous scholar's example about selling the slave for alimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi ruling on selling a slave for alimony and identify the error in Sadr al-Shari'a's example.

Core Idea
A slave sold for alimony can be sold again only for new debts, not the remainder of old ones.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq critiques a statement by Sadr al-Shari'a regarding the sale of a slave for alimony. The critique centers on whether the slave can be sold again for the remaining unpaid alimony. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi rules on recurring debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A slave owes alimony; the sale price does not cover the full debt. The question is whether he can be sold again for the remainder.
2
State Sadr al-Shari'a's position
As interpreted, he appears to allow a second sale for the remainder of the same debt.
The author quotes his ambiguous statement.
3
Recognize the author's critique
The author calls it a 'gross error' (sahw fahish) because it contradicts established Hanafi principles.
4
Understand the correct Hanafi principle
Alimony is a recurring debt; each new accumulation after the sale is a separate debt. The old debt becomes like mahr and cannot be collected by another sale.
The slave is sold only for what accrues during the buyer's ownership.
5
Apply the principle
Only new alimony debts authorize a second sale. Selling for the remainder of the old debt is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا يدل على أنه لو النفقة المجتمعة فلم يف بكلها فاشتراه بيع في من هو عالم به فإنه لا يباع لبقية النفقة الماضية لأنها حينئذ كالمهر وإنما يباع لما يجتمع من عند المشتري. وبهذا ظهر أن ما ذكره صدر الشريعة في شرح الوقاية من قوله صورته عبد النفقة عشرة أيام فانقضت بذلك عدتها هل يرجع عليها بما زاد على حصة العشرة أم لا؟ الجواب لا عندهما لا عند محمد وهو القياس . اهـ ملخصاً قوله : ( فينبغي أن يكون هنا كذلك) أقره عليه المقدسي وصاحب النهر قوله : لعدم صحة النكاح أراد بعدم الصحة عدم النفاذ وإلا فهو صحيح يتوقف نفاذه على إذن المولى قوله : (وأم الولد مثله في النهر والصواب وولد أم الولد.  ٣٢٦ كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة المنكوحة إنما تجب بالتبوأة والسكنى في بيت خال عن أهله وأهلها ولهم النظر والكلام بسبب دين تزوج امرأة بإذن المولى ففرض القاضي النفقة عليه فاجتمع عليه ألف درهم فبيع بخمسمائة وهي قيمته والمشتري عالم أن عليه دين النفقة يباع مرة أخرى بخلاف ما إذا كان الألف عليه آخر فبيع بخمسمائة لا يباع مرة أخرى .اهـ. سهو فاحش ظاهر لتصريحهم بأن النفقة في الحقيقة دين حادث عند المشتري، ولأنه يلزم عليه أن يكون دين النفقة أقوى من سائر الديون والأمر بالعكس
And this indicates that if the accumulated alimony is not fully covered by the sale price, and a person who knows about it buys him, then he is not sold for the remainder of the past alimony because it then becomes like mahr. He is only sold for what accumulates from the buyer. Thus it becomes clear that what Sadr al-Shari'a mentioned in Sharh al-Wiqaya – his statement: 'The form is a slave, alimony for ten days, and her idda ends with that; does he take recourse against her for what exceeds the share of the ten days?' The answer is no according to the two, but yes according to Muhammad, and it is qiyas. End summary. His statement: 'It should be similar here' he was approved by al-Maqdisi and the author of al-Nahr. His statement: 'Due to the invalidity of the marriage' he meant by invalidity non-enforceability; otherwise it is valid but its enforcement depends on the master's permission. His statement: 'And umm al-walad is like him in al-Nahr, and the correct view is the child of umm al-walad.' Page 326: The marriage of a woman is only obligatory by tabwi' and residence in a house free from his family and her family, and they have the right to see and speak about the debt. A man married a woman with the master's permission, and the judge fixed alimony on him, and 1000 dirhams accumulated on him, so he was sold for 500, which is his value, and the buyer knows that he has an alimony debt; he will be sold again. Contrary to if the 1000 were a later debt, he would not be sold again. End. This is an obvious and gross error because they explicitly stated that alimony is in reality a new debt for the buyer, and it would follow that alimony debt is stronger than other debts, whereas the opposite is true.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage critiques Sadr al-Shari'a's example regarding the sale of a slave for alimony. The correct ruling is that a slave can be sold again for new alimony debts accruing after the first sale, but not for the remainder of the old debt. The author emphasizes that alimony is a recurring debt that becomes new for each buyer. The critique points out a 'gross error' in Sadr al-Shari'a's statement that a slave sold for 1000 dirhams at a price of 500 can be sold again for the remaining 500; rather, he can only be sold for new alimony. The author notes that this would wrongly make alimony debt stronger than other debts.

Key Terms
صدر الشريعة (Sadr al-Shari'a): A Hanafi jurist (al-Mahbubi) who wrote Sharh al-Wiqaya.
سهو فاحش (sahw fahish): A gross error or oversight in legal reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Alimony is a recurring debt; a slave can be sold only for new alimony, not for the old arrears.

Three Exceptions to a Slave's Alimony Obligation
Lists three special exceptions to the alimony rule for a slave husband.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three exceptional cases where a slave is not responsible for his wife's alimony and apply the underlying reasoning to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
A slave's alimony obligation is excepted when the wife is also the master's property, when she is the master's daughter, and for her shroud.

The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that a husband must provide alimony to his wife, even if he is a slave. However, this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq outlines three important exceptions where the obligation does not rest on the slave but shifts to the master or is enforced differently. Understanding these exceptions clarifies how property ownership and familial relationships interact with marital duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first exception
Locate the case where the wife is a slave-girl owned by the same master as the slave husband. Note that this removes the slave's obligation completely.
Both are property of the same owner, so maintenance falls on the master.
2
Identify the second exception
Find the case where the wife is the master's daughter. She is entitled to alimony from her father's slave because her debt claim against her father extends to his property.
Alimony is analogous to a debt in this context.
3
Examine the third exception: the shroud
Read the discussion on the shroud of the slave's wife. Understand the qiyas (analogy) that the shroud is like clothing during life, so the husband is responsible.
The slave can be sold to cover the cost, per Imam Abu Yusuf's reasoning.
4
Synthesise the common principle
Each exception hinges on ownership or familial relation. The slave's obligation is removed or transferred when the wife is linked to the master financially or by blood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق المصنف في الزوجة فشمل الحرة والأمة ويستثنى من الأمة أمة سيد العبد فإنه لا نفقة لها على العبد بوأها العبد بيتاً أو لا، وإنما هي على المولى لأنهما جميعاً ملك المولى ونفقة المملوك على المالك؛ كذا في الذخيرة. وشمل بنت المولى فإن لها النفقة على عبد أبيها لأن النفقة في معنى سائر الديون من وجه والبنت تستحق الدين على الأب، وكذلك على عبد الأب؛ كذا في الذخيرة أيضاً. وقد سئلت عن كفن امرأة العبد وتجهيزها على القول المفتى به من أنه على الزوج وإن تركت مالاً فأجبت بأني إلى الآن لم أرها صريحة لكن تعليلهم لأبي يوسف بأن الكفن كالكسوة حال الحياة يقتضي أن يكون على العبد، ومقتضاه أن يباع فيه كما يباع في كسوتها .
And the author generalized in 'the wife' so it includes the free woman and the slave-girl. Yet the slave-girl of the slave's master is excluded: she has no alimony from the slave whether the slave provided her a home or not; rather it is upon the master because both are the property of the master, and the maintenance of a slave is upon the owner. This is in al-Dhakhira. And it includes the master's daughter: she is entitled to alimony from her father's slave because alimony is similar to other debts in some respects and the daughter can claim a debt from her father, and similarly from the father's slave. Also in al-Dhakhira. And I was asked about the shroud of the slave's wife and her burial preparation, according to the fatwa that it is upon the husband even if she leaves wealth. I answered that I have not yet seen it explicitly, but their reasoning for Abu Yusuf that the shroud is like clothing during life would require it to be upon the slave, and the consequence is that he can be sold for it just as he is sold for her clothing.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage outlines three special cases: (1) The slave-girl who is the property of the slave's own master: she is not entitled to alimony from the slave because both belong to the same master, so the master is responsible for her maintenance. (2) The daughter of the master who marries the master's slave: she is entitled to alimony from the slave because she can claim a debt from her father and from his property, including his slave. (3) The shroud of the slave's wife: based on Abu Yusuf's analogy that shroud is like clothing, the slave is responsible and can be sold to cover its cost.

Key Terms
أمة سيد العبد (amat sayyid al-'abd): A slave-girl owned by the same master who owns the slave husband.
الكفن (al-kafan): The shroud used to cover the deceased's body for burial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The slave's alimony obligation is removed or transferred in three specific cases: wife owned by master, wife is master's daughter, and expense for shroud.

Maintenance of a Married Slave-Girl: The Condition of Tabwi'
This passage explains the condition for maintenance of a married slave-girl and the definition of tabwi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the husband is obligated to provide maintenance for his married slave-wife and explain the role of tabwi'.

Core Idea
A slave-girl's husband must provide maintenance only when the master grants separate living, ensuring her full availability.

This lesson clarifies a unique Hanafi rule regarding the maintenance (nafaqa) of a married female slave. The key factor is not simply the marriage but the master's permission and, more specifically, the granting of a separate home (tabwi'). Without tabwi', the husband is not required to support his slave-wife. We also explore the exceptional case of the mukataba and nuances when the master uses her services after tabwi'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine what the passage rules: maintenance of a married slave-girl is wajib only upon tabwi'.
The Arabic phrase: 'ونفقة الأمة المنكوحة إنما تجب بالتبوأة'.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is tabwi' necessary? The text says 'لأنه لا احتباس إلا بها' — retention (availability) for the husband is the basis for maintenance.
The concept of احتباس (retention) is central.
3
Apply to slave categories
The ruling covers all types of female slaves (qinn, mudabbar, umm al-walad) except the mukataba. List which are included.
The text generalizes 'الأمة' to include these, but excludes mukataba.
4
Analyze exceptions and nuances
Study the mukataba exception and the detail about the master using the slave after tabwi' — when does maintenance drop?
The passage distinguishes between casual service and forcible prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ونفقة الأمة المنكوحة إنما تجب بالتبوأة لأنه لا احتباس إلا بها فإن بوأها المولى منزلاً فعليه النفقة لتحقق الاحتباس وإلا فلا لعدمه أطلق في الزوج فشمل الخر والقن والمدبر والمكاتب، وأطلق في الأمة فشمل القنة والمدبرة وأم الولد وأما المكاتبة فهي كالحرة ولا يحتاج إلى التبوأة لاستحقاق النفقة لأن منافعها على حكم ملكها بصيرورتها أحق بنفسها ومنافعها بعقد الكتابة ولهذا لم يبق للمولى ولاية الاستخدام فكانت كالحرة والتبوأة أن يخلي بين الأمة وزوجها في منزل الزوج ولا يستخدمها ؛ كذا في كافي الحاكم الشهيد وهو يفيد أنه لو جاءت الأمة من منزل زوجها بعد التبوأة وخدمت المولى في بعض الأوقات من غير أن يستخدمها لم يسقط كما صرح به في الذخيرة وفيها لو جاءت إلى بيت المولى في وقت والمولى ليس في البيت فاستخدمها أهله ومنعوها من الرجوع إلى بيته فلا نفقة لها لأن معه المولى
His statement: 'And the maintenance of the married slave-girl is only obligatory by tabwi' because there is no retention except by it. If the master grants her a home, then the maintenance is upon him (the husband) because retention is realized; otherwise not due to its absence. He generalized in 'husband' so it includes the khar, qinn, mudabbar, and mukatab. And he generalized in 'slave-girl' so it includes the qinn, mudabbar, and umm al-walad. As for the mukataba, she is like a free woman and does not need tabwi' for entitlement to maintenance because her benefits are under her ownership by her becoming more entitled to herself and her benefits through the kitaba contract, thus the master has no authority to use her, so she is like a free woman. Tabwi' is that the master leaves the slave-girl and her husband in the husband's home and does not use her. This is in Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid. It implies that if the slave-girl comes from her husband's home after tabwi' and serves the master at times without him using her, it does not drop. As stated in al-Dhakhira: if she comes to the master's house at a time while the master is not home, and his family uses her and prevent her from returning to her house, then she has no alimony because the master is not with her (i.e., not the one using her?).
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage explains that a married slave-girl (أمة منكوحة) is only entitled to maintenance from her husband if the master grants her tabwi', i.e., giving her a separate home away from the master's service. Without tabwi', the husband is not required to provide maintenance because the wife is not fully available to him. The ruling applies to various types of slaves, including qinn, mudabbar, and umm al-walad. A mukataba (slave under contract of manumission) is exempt from this condition because she is considered like a free woman due to her partial freedom. The text also clarifies that if the master allows tabwi' but then occasionally uses her services without her husband's consent, the maintenance is not dropped. However, if the master's family forcibly uses her and prevents her return, the maintenance ceases because the husband is not at fault.

Key Terms
التبوأة (al-tabwi'): The act of the master granting a married slave-girl a separate home from his household, freeing her for her husband's company.
المكاتبة (al-mukataba): A female slave under a contract of manumission (kitaba).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the master do for a married slave-girl to be entitled to maintenance from her husband?
A) Free her
B) Grant her a separate home (tabwi')
C) Write a contract of manumission
D) Give her a gift

Key Takeaway
Maintenance for a married slave-girl is not automatic; it requires the master's act of tabwi' to ensure her full availability to her husband.

Wife's Right to Visit Parents and Mahrams
Explains when a wife may exit the home without husband's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a wife's right to go out without her husband's permission and identify the exceptions based on the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A wife may visit parents weekly and mahrams yearly without permission; other outings require husband's consent.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on when a wife may leave the home without her husband's permission. The primary rule is that she must obey her husband, but Islamic law grants her the right to visit her parents and mahrams on a regular basis, even without his consent, to maintain family ties. We will also explore exceptions for emergencies and the condition of avoiding adornment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary rule and its proof
Read the opening lines where the text discusses a disabled father. Note that the wife must disobey her husband if he prevents her from serving her needy parent. This establishes that obedience to parents in necessity overrides spousal obedience.
This is a key principle: filial duty can supersede marital obedience.
2
Extract the mufta bihi ruling on periodic visits
Find the statement: 'tukhraj li al-walidain fi kulli jumu'ah bi-idhnihi wa bi-ghayr idhnihi, wa li ziyarat al-maharim fi kulli sanah marrah.' This is the relied-upon position.
The text explicitly calls this 'al-sahih al-mufta bihi'.
3
Note the exceptions for midwife, washer, and legal claims
Identify the categories: midwife (qaabilah), washer of the dead (ghaasilah), and anyone with a legal right. These can go out without permission due to necessity.
The text contrasts these with fard kifayah vs fard 'ayn.
4
Analyze the condition of avoiding adornment
The text cites Quran 33:33 to require that when a wife goes out, she must not display adornment that attracts men's looks. This is a universal condition.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah, so it supports a restriction but not a prohibition of going out entirely.
5
Examine the bathhouse dispute with Qadikhan
The text presents a disagreement: the faqih says she is prevented from the hammam, while Qadikhan holds it is legislated for both sexes. Note the thamarah al-khilaf and the tarjih.
The tarjih sides with prevention due to prevalent immodesty.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof from the Quran, discussing the wife's right to go out for specific purposes and the husband's authority to prevent her. The correct position (al-mufta bihi) is that a wife may visit her parents every Friday and her mahrams once a year, with or without her husband's permission. For additional visits to relatives, she needs his permission. The text cites various sources (Fath al-Qadir, al-Zahiriyyah, al-Khulasah) to establish these rules. It also details exceptions for midwives, washers of the dead, and women pursuing legal claims, who may go out without permission when necessary. For learning religious knowledge, the ruling depends on the husband's ability to teach. The author notes a disagreement with Qadikhan regarding women entering the bathhouse: while the faqih says she is prevented, Qadikhan holds that entering is legislated for both sexes, provided there is no exposure of 'awrah. The commentary emphasizes that the husband's right takes precedence over communal obligations (fard kifayah) but not over individual obligations (fard 'ayn) like Hajj. The condition of avoiding adornment when going out is supported by Quran 33:33.

Key Terms
المحارم (al-mahaarim): Relatives whom one is permanently forbidden to marry (e.g., brother, uncle, father).
الوالدين (al-walidayn): Both parents.
القابلة (al-qaabilah): Midwife; a woman who assists in childbirth.
الغاسلة (al-ghaasilah): A woman who washes deceased bodies (of women).
المخدرة (al-mukhadarah): A woman who is secluded and does not normally go out; she does not appear in public.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance; financial support due to wife and dependents.

Nafaqah Across Religions: The Exception for Spouse, Parents, and Children
Explaining the exception to the rule that nafaqah is not due across religions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah across religious differences and explain the proofs for the exceptions for spouse, parents, and children.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is wajib for spouse and lineal ascendants/descendants regardless of religion, based on contract and juz'iyyah.

The Hanafi school holds that maintenance (nafaqah) is generally tied to inheritance, but for close family relations, stronger proofs override religious differences. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the exceptions and their rationale, distinguishing between spouse, parents/children, and other relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
First, understand that nafaqah is normally wajib for a wife due to the marriage contract, and this does not depend on unity of religion. Then note that for blood relatives, the rule is tied to inheritance.
The spouse exception is straightforward; the parents/children exception requires deeper reasoning.
2
Analyze the juz'iyyah principle
The passage introduces the concept of 'juz'iyyah' (partiality): a child is considered a part of the parent. Therefore, maintaining a child is like maintaining oneself, and one's own disbelief does not prevent spending on oneself, so disbelief does not prevent spending on one's part.
This reasoning applies to both parents and children, as they are in the line of descent/ascent.
3
Examine the harbi exception
The exception to the exception: if the relative is a harbi (from a warring state), then nafaqah is not wajib, based on the hadith prohibiting kindness to those who fight us in religion.
This shows that the juz'iyyah principle is overridden by the prohibition of supporting enemies.
4
Differentiate between nafaqah and 'itq
The passage contrasts nafaqah (tied to inheritance by text) with emancipation upon ownership ('itq), which is tied to kinship and marital prohibition by hadith. This explains why nafaqah across religions is restricted to those who inherit (spouse, parents, children) while 'itq applies to a broader set of relatives.
Understanding this distinction clarifies why siblings are not included in the nafaqah exception.
5
Synthesize the final ruling
Combine all points: Nafaqah is wajib for spouse (by contract), parents, and children (by juz'iyyah and inheritance link), even across religions, except when they are harbi. For other relatives (e.g., siblings), nafaqah is not wajib when religions differ.
The passage concludes with al-Dhakhira's confirmation of this limited exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة وأبويه أحد ولقريب محرم فقير عاجز عن الكسب بقدر الإرث لو موسراً وصح بيع . واجبة لها بالعقد لاحتباسها بحق مقصود ،له وهذا لا يتعلق باتحاد الملة، وأما غيرها فلأن الجزئية ثابتة وجزء المرء في معنى نفسه فكما لا تمتنع نفقة نفسه بكفره لا تمتنع نفقة جزئه إلا أنهم إذا كانوا حربيين لا تجب نفقتهم على المسلم وإن كانوا متساويين لأنا نهينا عن البر في حق من يقاتلنا في الدين أطلق في الولاد فشمل الأبوين والأجداد والجدات والولد وولد الولد. وفي المستصفى صورنه تزوج ذمي ذمية وحصل لهما ولد ثم أسلمت الذمية حكم بإسلام الولد تبعاً لها والنفقة على الأب . وهذا قبل عروض الإسلام، ويحتمل أن يعتقد الكفر في صغره وكفره صحيح عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد اهـ وقيد بالزوجية والولاد لأن فيما عدا ذلك لا تجب مع اختلاف الدين فلا يجب على المسلم نفقة أخيه النصراني وعكسه لأن النفقة متعلقة بالإرث بالنص بخلاف العتق عند الملك لأنه متعلق بالقرابة والمحرمية بالحديث، ولأن القرابة موجبة للصلة ومع الاتفاق في الدين أكد و دوام ملك اليمين أعلى في القطية من حرمان النفقة فاعتبرنا في الأصل أصل العلة وفي الأدنى العلة المؤكدة فلهذا افترقا . وفي الذخيرة البرهانية : ولا يجبر المسلم على نفقة الكافر من قرابته ولا الكافر على المسلم من قرابته إلا الزوجة والوالدين والولد ا.هـ.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance. And his parents one, and for a poor, disabled from earning close relative, proportional to inheritance, if wealthy and the sale is valid. It is wajib for her by the contract due to her confinement for a rightful purpose. And this is not related to unity of religion. As for other than [spouse], because the partiality is established, and the part of a person is in the meaning of himself; so just as maintenance of himself is not prevented by his disbelief, the maintenance of his part is not prevented, except if they are warring, then their maintenance is not obligatory upon the Muslim even if they are equal [in religion], because we have been prohibited from kindness towards those who fight us in religion. He mentioned generally for lineage, so it includes parents, grandparents, and children grandchildren. And in al-Mustasfa: its picture: a dhimmi man married a dhimmi woman and they had a child, then the dhimmi woman became Muslim, and the child's Islam is ruled following her, and the maintenance is on the father. This is before the father's Islam, and it is possible that the child believes in disbelief in his childhood, and his disbelief is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. End. And it is restricted to marriage and lineage, because beyond that it is not obligatory with difference of religion. So it is not obligatory for a Muslim to maintain his Christian brother, and vice versa. Because maintenance is connected to inheritance by text, unlike emancipation upon ownership because it is connected to kinship and marital prohibition by hadith. And because kinship obligates connection, and with agreement in religion it is strengthened, and the continuation of ownership is higher in giving than depriving maintenance. So we considered in the origin the primary cause, and in the lower the confirmed cause; thus they differ. And in al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya: A Muslim is not compelled to maintain a disbelieving relative, nor a disbeliever to maintain a Muslim relative, except for the spouse, parents, and child. End.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position on maintenance (nafaqah) across religious differences. The commentary opens by stating the general rule: maintenance is wajib for the wife due to the marriage contract, and this does not depend on unity of religion. For blood relatives (parents, children, etc.), the reasoning is based on the concept of 'juz'iyyah' (partiality): a child is considered a part of the parent, so just as one is not prevented from spending on oneself due to disbelief, one is not prevented from spending on one's child. However, an exception is made for harbis (those from a warring state) because of the hadith prohibiting kindness to those who fight us in religion. The passage then quotes from al-Mustasfa a case: a dhimmi couple, the wife converts to Islam, the child follows her in Islam, and the father (non-Muslim) is obligated to maintain the child. This is before the father's conversion. The commentary also clarifies that the child's disbelief in his childhood is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Then it restricts the exception to spouses and lineal ascendants/descendants: for other relatives (e.g., siblings), maintenance is not obligatory when religions differ. The reason given is that nafaqah is tied to inheritance (by Qur'anic text) rather than to kinship or marital prohibition (which is based on hadith). The passage concludes by quoting al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya: no one is compelled to maintain a relative of a different religion except the spouse, parents, and child.

Key Terms
الجزئية (al-juz'iyyah): The concept that a child is a 'part' (juz') of the parent, so the parent's maintenance of the child is akin to maintaining oneself.
الحربية (al-harbiyyah): A disbeliever from a warring state (dar al-harb), not a dhimmi.
العتق عند الملك (al-'itq 'inda al-milk): Emancipation of a slave upon ownership, which in Hanafi law is based on kinship and marital prohibition (hadith), not on inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which relatives are exempt from the rule that nafaqah is not obligated across religious differences?
A) Spouse, parents, and children
B) Ascendants and descendants except parents
C) Only children
D) All relatives except those who are warring

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, nafaqah is wajib for spouse, parents, and children even if they are of a different religion, except when they are harbis (from a warring state).

The Validity of Conditional Manumission: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explains the validity of conditional manumission and the Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on conditional manumission added to future ownership, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on the nature of isqat.

Core Idea
Manumission is isqat (elimination of ownership), so suspension is valid; transfer (tamlik) cannot be suspended.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding conditional manumission. The Hanafi school, relying on the concept of isqat, allows suspending manumission on future ownership, while Shafi'i considers such suspension invalid. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the Hanafi justification and presents examples to clarify the rule, including subtle subcases from Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on adding manumission to future ownership
Identify the ruling: the suspension is valid and manumission occurs upon acquisition of ownership. Note the key principle: manumission as isqat allows suspension.
The text explicitly says 'if he adds it to ownership... it is valid'.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
The passage mentions disagreement with al-Shafi'i. Recall that Shafi'i holds such suspension invalid because manumission cannot be suspended on an uncertain event.
Shafi'i likely treats manumission as tamlik (transfer) which requires immediate effect.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi proof is talil: manumission is isqat (elimination of ownership), and isqat permits suspension, unlike tamlik. This is supported by analogy to divorce (which is also isqat).
The text states: 'because it is an elimination (isqat) so suspension applies to it, unlike transfers of ownership (tamlikat)'.
4
Examine the scope and exceptions
Not all future events are valid conditions. Valid: cause of ownership (purchase), time. Invalid: death of heir (not a cause of ownership). Special cases: tadbir (death of master) and istilad are valid exceptions.
The text distinguishes 'adding to a cause of ownership' from 'adding to death of an heir'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi, if a master says to a third party's slave 'If I buy you, you are free' and later buys him, the slave is freed. Under Shafi'i, the condition is void and the slave is not freed; the master may keep him or free him separately.
This difference affects the legal status of the slave and the master's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن أضافه إلى ملك أو شرط صح) أي إن أضاف العتق إلى ملك بأن قال إن ملكتك فأنت حر أو إلى شرط كقوله لعبده إن دخلت الدار فأنت حر فإنه يصح ويقع العتق إذا وجد الشرط، أما الإضافة إلى الملك ففيه خلاف الشافعي وقد بيناه في كتاب الطلاق. وأما التعليق بالشرط فلأنه إسقاط فيجري فيه التعليق بخلاف التمليكات على ما عرف والإضافة إلى سبب الملك كإضافة إلى الملك كإن اشتريتك فأنت حر بخلاف إن مات مورثي فأنت ލ يصح لأن الموت لم يوضع سبباً للملك فالإضافة إلى وقت كالتعليق بالشرط من حيث إن الحكم لا يوجد فيهما إلا بعد وجود الشرط والوقت والمحل قبل ذلك على حكم ملك المالك في جميع الأحكام إلا في التعليق بشرط الموت المطلق وهو التدبير وكذا الاستيلاد؛ كذا في البدائع والتعليق بأمر كائن تنجيز قال في الظهيرية : لو قال لعبده إن حر عتق للحال بخلاف قوله لمكاتبه إن أنت فأنت عبدي ލ لا يعتق . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وبه نأخذ لأن في الإضافة قصوراً .اهـ. وفيها أيضاً: رجل قال لعبد رجل إن وهبك مولاك لي فأنت حر فوهبه له والعبد في يد الواهب لا يعتق قبل أو لم يقبل، وكذا لو كان العبد في يد الموهوب له وقد ابتدأ الواهب بالهبة قبل الموهوب له أو لم يقبل، وإن ابتدأ الموهوب له فقال هب لي هذا العبد والعبد في يد الموهوب له فقال صاحب العبد وهبت لك عتق اهـ. ومن مسائل التعليق اللطيفة ما في الظهيرية رجل قال لأمته إذا مات والدي فأنت حرة ثم باعها من والده ثم تزوجها ثم قال لها إن مات والدي فأنت طالق ثنتين فمات الوالد كان محمد رحمه الله تعالى يقول أولاً تعتق ولا تطلق ثم رجع وقال : لا يقع طلاق ولا عتاق والمسألة على الاستقصاء في المبسوط اهـ.
His statement: (And if he adds it to ownership or a condition, it is valid) i.e., if he adds manumission to ownership by saying, 'If I come to own you, you are free,' or to a condition like his statement to his slave, 'If you enter the house, you are free,' then it is valid and the manumission occurs when the condition is found. As for adding to ownership, there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, and we have explained it in the Book of Divorce. As for suspension upon a condition, because it is an elimination (isqat) so suspension applies to it, unlike transfers of ownership (tamlikat) as known. And adding to a cause of ownership is like adding to ownership, such as 'If I buy you, you are free,' unlike 'If my heir dies, you are free' – it is not valid because death is not set as a cause for ownership. So adding to a time is like suspension upon a condition in that the ruling does not exist except after the existence of the condition and time, and the subject before that remains under the owner's ownership in all rulings, except in suspension upon absolute death, which is tadbir, and similarly istilad; thus in al-Bada'i. And suspension upon an existing matter is immediate (tanjiz). Al-Zahiriya said: If one says to his slave, 'If you are free,' then he is free immediately, unlike his statement to his mukatab, 'If you are, you are my slave' – he does not become free. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: We take this because in the addition there is deficiency. End. Also in it: A man said to another man's slave, 'If your master gives you to me as a gift, you are free,' then he gave him as a gift, and the slave is in the giver's hand – he is not free whether or not the donee accepted. Similarly, if the slave was in the donee's hand and the giver initiated the gift before the donee accepted or not. And if the donee initiated saying, 'Give me this slave as a gift,' and the slave is in the donee's hand, and the owner said, 'I have given him to you as a gift,' then he becomes free. End. Among the subtle issues of suspension is in al-Zahiriyya: A man said to his slave girl, 'When my father dies, you are free.' Then he sold her to his father, then married her, then said to her, 'If my father dies, you are divorced twice.' Then the father died. Muhammad (rahimahullah) first said: She is free and not divorced. Then he retracted and said: Neither divorce nor manumission occurs. The issue is detailed in al-Mabsut. End.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling on conditional manumission. The commentator begins by clarifying the matn's statement: 'If he adds it to ownership or a condition, it is valid.' This means that if a master says to a slave he does not yet own, 'If I come to own you, you are free,' or if he says to his own slave, 'If you enter the house, you are free,' the suspension is valid and manumission takes effect upon fulfillment of the condition. The commentator notes that the case of adding manumission to future ownership is disputed with al-Shafi'i, and refers to the earlier discussion in the Book of Divorce for details. The justification given for allowing suspension on conditions is that manumission is an 'isqat' (elimination of ownership) and thus suspension is permissible, unlike transfer of ownership (tamlik) where suspension is not valid. Adding to a cause of ownership (like purchase) is treated as adding to ownership itself, so it is valid. However, adding to mere death of an heir is not a cause of ownership, so it is not valid. Adding to a time is like suspension on a condition; the ruling occurs after the time passes, but until then the slave remains in the owner's full ownership. Exceptions are tadbir (manumission after death) and istilad (manumission of a slave mother upon her owner's death). The commentator then presents various rulings from al-Zahiriyya regarding suspension, including the effect of adding the word 'free' as a condition or the statement to a mukatab. The passage ends with a subtle case narrated by Imam Muhammad involving a slave girl and multiple conditional statements, illustrating the complexities of suspension.

Key Terms
إسقاط (isqat): Elimination of a right or ownership; manumission is considered isqat of the master's ownership over the slave.
تدبير (tadbir): Manumission suspended on the master's death; the slave becomes a mudabbar and is freed after the master's death.
استيلاد (istilad): Manumission of a slave mother upon the death of her owner when she has given birth to his child.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; something that cannot be suspended, unlike isqat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a master says to a slave he does not own, 'If I buy you, you are free'?
A) The statement is invalid because manumission cannot be suspended.
B) It is valid and the slave becomes free upon purchase.
C) It is valid only if the slave consents.
D) It is invalid because the master does not own the slave yet.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, manumission is isqat, which permits suspension on future ownership or conditions, unlike tamlik; this distinguishes Hanafi from Shafi'i rulings.

Service of a Shared Umm Walad: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on distribution of service in shared Umm Walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on distribution of service in a shared Umm Walad when one owner acknowledges the child, and explain the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
In a shared Umm Walad, the service goes to the denier wholly or in half, never to the acknowledger.

This passage deals with a nuanced case: two men jointly own an Umm Walad (a slave woman who bears a child from her master). One partner acknowledges the child as his own; the other denies. How is her service apportioned? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (after retraction) agree on a rule based on certainty, while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds a different view. Let's examine the reasoning and the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Distinguish between the muqirr (acknowledger) and the munkir (denier). The muqirr claims paternity; the munkir denies. A third party (shahid) may be present as a witness.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's two cases
Abu Hanifa rules: if the muqirr is truthful, all service goes to the munkir; if he is lying, half the service goes to the munkir. This ensures that at least half service is certain for the munkir.
The concept of certainty (al-matiqan bihi) is used.
3
Analyze the legal consequences
The muqirr's acknowledgment of the child includes acknowledgment of lineage (nasab), which is binding and cannot be revoked. Therefore, the muqirr cannot be treated as a mustawlid (one who begets a slave child) to give him service rights.
4
Examine the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially differed but retracted to Abu Hanifa's view (as reported by Al-Hakim). Only Muhammad al-Shaybani maintained that no one has the right to use her service.
Thus the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's.
5
Determine the final ruling
The mufta bihi: if the muqirr is truthful, all service to the munkir; if lying, half service to the munkir. The witness (shahid) gets no service and no sa'y (earning) is imposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك على ملكه في الحكم فتخرج إلى العناق بالسعاية كأم ولد النصراني إذا أسلمت. ولأبي حنيفة أن المقر لو صدق كانت الخدمة كلها للمنكر ولو كذب كان له نصف الخدمة فيثبت ما هو المتيقن به وهو النصف ولا خدمة للشريك الشاهد ولا استسعاء لأنه يبرأ عن جميع بدعوى الاستيلاد والضمان والإقرار بأمومية الولد يتضمن الإقرار بالنسب وهو أمر لازم لا يرتد بالرد فلا يمكن أن يجعل المقر كالمستولد. ونص الحاكم في الكافي على أن أبا يوسف رجع إلى قول أبي حنيفة فالمخالف فيها محمد فقط، وعلى قوله ليس لأحد أن يستخدمها.
That is in his ownership in legal judgment, so she goes out to nursing with seeking earnings like the Umm Walad of a Christian when she becomes Muslim. According to Abu Hanifa, if the acknowledging one were truthful, all service would be for the denier, and if he lied, half the service would be for him, so what is certain is established, which is half, and the partner who is a witness has no service and no seeking earnings because he is absolved from all by the claim of bearing a child and the guarantee, and acknowledging the motherhood of the child includes acknowledging lineage, which is binding and cannot be revoked by denial, so the acknowledging one cannot be made like a mustawlid. Al-Hakim in al-Kafi stated that Abu Yusuf retracted to Abu Hanifa's view, so only Muhammad differed with him, and according to him no one has the right to use her service.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where two men jointly own an Umm Walad (a slave woman who bears a child from her master). One of them (the muqirr) acknowledges that the child is his, while the other (the munkir) denies it. Abu Hanifa states that if the muqirr is truthful, the entire service of the slave goes to the munkir. If he is lying, half the service goes to the munkir. Thus half is established with certainty. The partner who is a witness (shahid) gets no service, nor does the woman have to seek earnings (sa'y). This is because the muqirr absolves himself by claiming the child and the guarantee (daman). The acknowledgment of the child's maternity implies acknowledgment of lineage, which is binding and cannot be rejected. Therefore the muqirr cannot be treated like a mustawlid. The text notes that al-Hakim in al-Kafi states that Abu Yusuf retracted to Abu Hanifa's view, so only Muhammad al-Shaybani differed. According to Muhammad, no one has the right to use her service at all.

Key Terms
المقر (al-muqirr): The owner who acknowledges that the child born to the shared Umm Walad is his.
المنكر (al-munkir): The owner who denies that the child is his.
الخدمة (al-khidmah): The service or labor of the slave woman, which is divided between the co-owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The service goes to the denier: all or half, depending on truthfulness.
D) The Umm Walad is freed and no service is owed.

Key Takeaway
In a shared Umm Walad, the denier receives the service in full or half based on certainty, while the acknowledger never benefits from the service.

Earnings and Maintenance of a Shared Umm Walad
Details on earnings and maintenance of a shared Umm Walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on dividing earnings and determining maintenance for a shared Umm Walad, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Earnings split: half to denier, half suspended; maintenance from her earnings or half on denier per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the rulings a Hanafi jurist must know when a shared Umm Walad is involved. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq omitted these details, but they are critical for resolving disputes. We focus first on her earnings and maintenance, where Abu Hanifa and Muhammad differ on the denier's obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the acknowledging partner (muqir) and the denier (munkir). After the muqir's acknowledgment, the Umm Walad is considered half-owned by each.
The muqir’s acknowledgment is binding only on himself.
2
Determine the ownership status
The muqir’s claim that she is his Umm Walad makes her status disputed. The denier’s share remains a slave; the muqir’s share is as Umm Walad.
The denier’s share is a regular slave until proven otherwise.
3
Apply the rule for earnings
Earnings from her labor (kasb) follow the benefit (manafi') of the slave. Since the denier owns half the benefit, half her earnings go to him; the other half is suspended until the claim is resolved.
This is based on the principle that benefits follow ownership.
4
Apply the rule for maintenance
Maintenance (nafaqah) is first taken from her earnings. If she has no earnings, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa says half maintenance is on the denier because he owns half; Muhammad says none because he has no service or detention rights.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa’s, as per Fath al-Qadir.
5
Conclude with tarjih
Fath al-Qadir adopts Abu Hanifa’s view as befitting his principles. The denier must pay half her maintenance if she has no earnings.
This is the operative fatwa whenever the ownership is disputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقر فلأنه تبرأ منها بالدعوى على شريكه وأما المنكر فلأنه لما أنكر نفذ الإقرار على المقر فصار كإقراره أنه استولدها، ثم إذا أدت نصف قيمتها إلى المنكر عتق كلها لأن العتق لا يتجزئ عندهما، ولم يذكر المصنف حكم كسبها ونفقتها وجنايتها والجناية عليها وحكمها بعد موت أحدهما . أما الأول ففي غاية البيان نصف كسبها للمنكر ونصفه موقوف اعتباراً بمنافعها . وأما نفقتها فمن كسبها فإن لم يكن لها كسب ففي المختلف في باب محمد أن نفقتها على المنكر ولم يذكر خلافاً. وقال غيره : إن النصف على المنكر لأن نصف الجارية له. قال في فتح القدير : وهو اللائق بقول أبي حنيفة. وينبغي على قول محمد أن لا نفقة لها عليه أصلاً لأنه لا خدمة له عليها ولا احتباس وأما جنايتها والجناية عليها فموقوفة عند الإمام إلى تصديق أحدهما صاحبه، وعلى قول أبي يوسف أولاً وهو قول محمد تسعى في جنايتها بمنزلة المكاتب وتأخذ أرش الجناية عليها فتستعين به كما في الكافي للحاكم وتبعه في غاية البيان وفتح القدير، وقد نقل الزيلعي أن النصف موقوف والنصف على الجاحد عند الإمام، وفي صحته عن الإمام نظر لما علمت أن مذهبه التوقف في الكل. وفي المحيط : وذكر محمد التوقف على الإطلاق وهو الصحيح لأنه تعذر إيجاب يوجب الجناية في نصيب المنكر على المنكر لأنه عجز عن دفعها بالجناية من غير صنع منه فلا تلزمه الفدية كما لو أبق أو مات بعد الجناية بخلاف الجناية عليها لأنه أمكن دفع نصيب الأرش إلى المنكر، سواء كان نصيبه قناً أو أم ولد فلا معنى للتوقف ا هـ . وأما إذا مات المنكر فإنها تعتق لإقرار المقر أنها كانت كأم ولد له ثم تسعى في نصف قيمتها لورثة المنكر، ولا تسعى للمقر لأنه يدعي الضمان دون السعاية ولم أر حكمها إذا مات المقر لظهور أن الأمر كما كان قبل موته فتخدم المنكر يوماً وتتوقف يوماً.
As for the acknowledging one, because he absolves himself by the claim against his partner; and as for the denier, because when he denied, the acknowledgment is effective against the acknowledger, so it becomes like his acknowledgment that he caused her to bear, then when she pays half her value to the denier, she is entirely emancipated because emancipation is indivisible according to them. The author did not mention the ruling on her earnings, maintenance, her tort, tort against her, and her ruling after one of them dies. As for the first, in Ghayat al-Bayan, half her earnings go to the denier and half are suspended according to her benefits. As for her maintenance, it is from her earnings; if she has no earnings, then in al-Mukhtalif in the chapter of Muhammad that her maintenance is on the denier and he did not mention a difference. Others said: half is on the denier because half the slave is his. He said in Fath al-Qadir: it is befitting of Abu Hanifa's view. And it should be according to Muhammad's view that there is no maintenance on him at all because he has no service from her and no detention. As for her tort and tort against her, they are suspended according to the Imam until one of them confirms the other. According to the first view of Abu Yusuf, which is Muhammad's view, she seeks earnings for her tort like a mukatab and takes the compensation for tort against her and uses it for help, as in al-Kafi by al-Hakim, followed by Ghayat al-Bayan and Fath al-Qadir. Al-Zayla'i reported that half is suspended and half on the denier according to the Imam, and its correctness from the Imam is questionable because you know that his madhhab is suspension in all. In al-Muhit: Muhammad mentioned suspension absolutely and it is correct because it is impossible to impose a tort in the share of the denier on the denier because he is unable to pay it by the tort without his action, so the fidyah is not incumbent on him, as if he ran away or died after the tort, contrary to tort against her because it is possible to pay the share of the compensation to the denier, whether his share is a slave or an Umm Walad, so there is no meaning for suspension. As for when the denier dies, she is emancipated due to the acknowledger's acknowledgment that she was like his Umm Walad, then she seeks earnings for half her value for the denier's heirs, and she does not seek earnings for the acknowledger because he claims the guarantee without seeking earnings. I have not seen her ruling if the acknowledger dies, because it appears that the matter remains as it was before his death, so she serves the denier one day and suspends one day.

Commentary
This passage explains rulings that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq omitted. First, regarding earnings: half go to the denier, half are suspended. Maintenance is from her earnings; if none, then according to the relied-upon view (Abu Hanifa), half is on the denier. According to Muhammad, no maintenance on anyone because he has no service rights. For her tort (jinnayah) and tort against her, the Imam (Abu Hanifa) holds suspension until one owner confirms the other's claim. Abu Yusuf's initial view (and Muhammad's) treats her like a mukatab: she must earn to pay compensation for her tort and receives compensation for tort against her. Al-Zayla'i reports that half is suspended and half on the denier per Abu Hanifa, but this is questioned. The text prefers Muhammad's view of absolute suspension because the denier cannot be held liable for tort in his share without his fault. If the denier dies, she is freed due to the acknowledger's acknowledgment, then earns half her value for the denier's heirs. If the acknowledger dies, she continues serving the denier one day and suspending one day.

Key Terms
الكسب (al-kasb): Earnings of the Umm Walad from her labor, half of which goes to the denier and half suspended.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance of the Umm Walad, due from her earnings or from the denier if she has no earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The denier pays full maintenance.
B) The denier pays half maintenance.
C) The acknowledger pays half maintenance.
D) No one is obligated to pay maintenance.

Key Takeaway
For a shared Umm Walad with no earnings, the denier must pay half her maintenance per the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Torts and Death Rulings for a Shared Umm Walad
Details on torts and death rulings of a shared Umm Walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi views on the treatment of torts (jinnayah) committed by or against a shared Umm Walad, and the changes upon the death of one partner.

Core Idea
For torts, absolute suspension (Muhammad) is mufta bihi; upon denier's death, she is freed and must seek earnings.

This passage continues to fill in the omissions from the matn. A shared Umm Walad can commit a tort (jinayah) or be a victim of one. There is a significant intra-Hanafi dispute on how liability is assigned. Additionally, the death of one partner drastically changes her status. Understanding these rules is essential for practicing Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of tort
Distinguish between a tort committed by her (jinayah minha) and a tort committed against her (jinayah alayha). Each has different implications.
The passage discusses both directions.
2
Analyze tort committed by her
Abu Hanifa holds full suspension until one partner confirms the other's claim. Abu Yusuf (first view) and Muhammad hold that she is treated like a mukatab: she must seek earnings (sa'y) to pay compensation.
The relied-upon view is Muhammad's absolute suspension, per al-Muhit.
3
Analyze tort committed against her
According to the same sources, compensation for tort against her is given to her to use for help (e.g., to pay her own tort fines). This is also subject to suspension according to the Imam.
Ghayat al-Bayan and Fath al-Qadir follow this view.
4
Examine death of the denier
If the denier dies, the muqir's acknowledgment takes effect: she is freed (because she was his Umm Walad). Then she must seek earnings to pay half her value to the denier's heirs.
She does not seek earnings for the muqir because he claims guarantee without seeking earnings.
5
Examine death of the acknowledger
The author has not seen a ruling on this. Apparently, the situation remains as before: she serves the denier one day and suspends one day.
This reflects the ongoing dispute.
