أما المقر فلأنه تبرأ منها بالدعوى على شريكه وأما المنكر فلأنه لما أنكر نفذ الإقرار على المقر فصار كإقراره أنه استولدها، ثم إذا أدت نصف قيمتها إلى المنكر عتق كلها لأن العتق لا يتجزئ عندهما، ولم يذكر المصنف حكم كسبها ونفقتها وجنايتها والجناية عليها وحكمها بعد موت أحدهما . أما الأول ففي غاية البيان نصف كسبها للمنكر ونصفه موقوف اعتباراً بمنافعها . وأما نفقتها فمن كسبها فإن لم يكن لها كسب ففي المختلف في باب محمد أن نفقتها على المنكر ولم يذكر خلافاً. وقال غيره : إن النصف على المنكر لأن نصف الجارية له. قال في فتح القدير : وهو اللائق بقول أبي حنيفة. وينبغي على قول محمد أن لا نفقة لها عليه أصلاً لأنه لا خدمة له عليها ولا احتباس وأما جنايتها والجناية عليها فموقوفة عند الإمام إلى تصديق أحدهما صاحبه، وعلى قول أبي يوسف أولاً وهو قول محمد تسعى في جنايتها بمنزلة المكاتب وتأخذ أرش الجناية عليها فتستعين به كما في الكافي للحاكم وتبعه في غاية البيان وفتح القدير، وقد نقل الزيلعي أن النصف موقوف والنصف على الجاحد عند الإمام، وفي صحته عن الإمام نظر لما علمت أن مذهبه التوقف في الكل. وفي المحيط : وذكر محمد التوقف على الإطلاق وهو الصحيح لأنه تعذر إيجاب يوجب الجناية في نصيب المنكر على المنكر لأنه عجز عن دفعها بالجناية من غير صنع منه فلا تلزمه الفدية كما لو أبق أو مات بعد الجناية بخلاف الجناية عليها لأنه أمكن دفع نصيب الأرش إلى المنكر، سواء كان نصيبه قناً أو أم ولد فلا معنى للتوقف ا هـ . وأما إذا مات المنكر فإنها تعتق لإقرار المقر أنها كانت كأم ولد له ثم تسعى في نصف قيمتها لورثة المنكر، ولا تسعى للمقر لأنه يدعي الضمان دون السعاية ولم أر حكمها إذا مات المقر لظهور أن الأمر كما كان قبل موته فتخدم المنكر يوماً وتتوقف يوماً.
As for the acknowledging one, because he absolves himself by the claim against his partner; and as for the denier, because when he denied, the acknowledgment is effective against the acknowledger, so it becomes like his acknowledgment that he caused her to bear, then when she pays half her value to the denier, she is entirely emancipated because emancipation is indivisible according to them. The author did not mention the ruling on her earnings, maintenance, her tort, tort against her, and her ruling after one of them dies. As for the first, in Ghayat al-Bayan, half her earnings go to the denier and half are suspended according to her benefits. As for her maintenance, it is from her earnings; if she has no earnings, then in al-Mukhtalif in the chapter of Muhammad that her maintenance is on the denier and he did not mention a difference. Others said: half is on the denier because half the slave is his. He said in Fath al-Qadir: it is befitting of Abu Hanifa's view. And it should be according to Muhammad's view that there is no maintenance on him at all because he has no service from her and no detention. As for her tort and tort against her, they are suspended according to the Imam until one of them confirms the other. According to the first view of Abu Yusuf, which is Muhammad's view, she seeks earnings for her tort like a mukatab and takes the compensation for tort against her and uses it for help, as in al-Kafi by al-Hakim, followed by Ghayat al-Bayan and Fath al-Qadir. Al-Zayla'i reported that half is suspended and half on the denier according to the Imam, and its correctness from the Imam is questionable because you know that his madhhab is suspension in all. In al-Muhit: Muhammad mentioned suspension absolutely and it is correct because it is impossible to impose a tort in the share of the denier on the denier because he is unable to pay it by the tort without his action, so the fidyah is not incumbent on him, as if he ran away or died after the tort, contrary to tort against her because it is possible to pay the share of the compensation to the denier, whether his share is a slave or an Umm Walad, so there is no meaning for suspension. As for when the denier dies, she is emancipated due to the acknowledger's acknowledgment that she was like his Umm Walad, then she seeks earnings for half her value for the denier's heirs, and she does not seek earnings for the acknowledger because he claims the guarantee without seeking earnings. I have not seen her ruling if the acknowledger dies, because it appears that the matter remains as it was before his death, so she serves the denier one day and suspends one day.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage explains rulings that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq omitted. First, regarding earnings: half go to the denier, half are suspended. Maintenance is from her earnings; if none, then according to the relied-upon view (Abu Hanifa), half is on the denier. According to Muhammad, no maintenance on anyone because he has no service rights. For her tort (jinnayah) and tort against her, the Imam (Abu Hanifa) holds suspension until one owner confirms the other's claim. Abu Yusuf's initial view (and Muhammad's) treats her like a mukatab: she must earn to pay compensation for her tort and receives compensation for tort against her. Al-Zayla'i reports that half is suspended and half on the denier per Abu Hanifa, but this is questioned. The text prefers Muhammad's view of absolute suspension because the denier cannot be held liable for tort in his share without his fault. If the denier dies, she is freed due to the acknowledger's acknowledgment, then earns half her value for the denier's heirs. If the acknowledger dies, she continues serving the denier one day and suspending one day.

Key Terms
الجناية (al-jinayah): Tort committed by the Umm Walad or against her; ruling is suspended or requires sa'y.
السعاية (al-sa'yah): Seeking earnings; the act of earning to pay off a debt or compensation, like a mukatab does.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in al-Muhit, how is a tort committed by a shared Umm Walad handled?
A) She is treated like a free person and pays full compensation.
B) She is treated like a mukatab and must seek earnings to pay.
C) Liability is absolutely suspended because the denier cannot be held responsible without his action.
D) Half the compensation is due from each owner immediately.

Key Takeaway
For torts, the relied-upon Hanafi position is absolute suspension; upon the denier's death, she is freed and must seek earnings for half her value.

The Value (Taqawwum) of Umm Walad: Abu Hanifa vs. the Two Companions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on taqawwum with mudabbar analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether an Umm Walad is legally valuable (mutaqawwima), and apply the reasoning to distinguish between Umm Walad and mudabbar.

Core Idea
An Umm Walad has no value for compensation because she is possessed for lineage, not for assessment.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does an Umm Walad (a slave mother of her master's child) have legal value (taqawwum) for purposes of compensation? Abu Hanifa holds she does not, because her possession (ihraz) is for lineage (nasab), not for assessment. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that she does, because benefit can be derived from her and the prohibition of sale does not negate value. Understanding this dispute clarifies liability rules and the role of usul principles in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the taqawwum of Umm Walad
Identify which view is attributed to Abu Hanifa and which to the Sahibayn. Note that the dispute is about the legal status (hukm wad'i) of being mutaqawwim, not about permissibility of sale.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
The Sahibayn argue that the Umm Walad is mutaqawwima because benefit can be derived from her (intercourse, hire, service), and the prohibition of sale does not negate value, as seen in the mudabbar (a slave whose emancipation is deferred). They also cite the case of a Christian's Umm Walad who must seek earnings upon conversion.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response and distinguishing factor
Abu Hanifa responds that the Umm Walad's possession (ihraz) is for lineage, not for assessment. Possession for assessment is subsidiary. Therefore, she cannot be required to seek earnings for creditors or heirs, unlike a mudabbar. The lineage-based freedom (through her child) takes precedence, nullifying taqawwum.
Key distinction: the cause (sabab) for dropping assessment is present for Umm Walad but delayed for mudabbar.
4
Examine the mudabbar comparison and its resolution
The text resolves an apparent contradiction in al-Hidayah: tadbir (deferred emancipation) is considered a cause after death for dropping assessment, but a cause in the present for prohibiting sale. The response explains that tadbir's effect on sale prohibition is immediate due to necessity, but its effect on dropping assessment is delayed to after death because legal capacity ends at death.
This explains why mudabbar has value (taqawwum) while Umm Walad does not.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, no compensation is due if an Umm Walad is destroyed or injured. Under the Sahibayn, compensation would be required based on her market value. Also, under Abu Hanifa, the Umm Walad cannot be compelled to seek earnings for debts; under the Sahibayn, she can be.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is Abu Hanifa's.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما لأم ولد تقوم) أي ليس لها قيمة عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : إنها متقومة للانتقاع بها وطئاً وإجارة واستخداماً، وهذا هو التقوم، وبامتناع بيعها لا يسقط تقومها كما في المدير ألا ترى أن أم ولد النصراني إذا أسلمت عليها السعاية وهذا آية التقوم غير أن قيمتها ثلث قيمتها قنه على ما قالوا لفوات البيع، والسعاية بعد الموت بخلاف المدبر لفوات منفعة البيع، أما السعاية والاستخدام باقيان. ولأبي حنيفة أن التقوم بالإحراز وهي محرزة للنسب لا للتقوم والإحراز للتقوم تابع ولهذا لا تسعى لغريم ولا لوارث بخلاف المدبر . وهذا لأن النسب فيها متحقق في الحال وهو الحرية الثابتة بواسطة الولد على ما عرف في حرمة المصاهرة إلا أنه لم يظهر عمله في حق الملك ضرورة الانتقاع فعمل التسبب في إسقاط التقوم وفي المدير ينعقد السبب بعد الموت وامتناع البيع فيه لتحقق مقصوده فافترقا وفي أم ولد النصراني قضينا بكتابتها عليه دفعاً للضرر من الجانبين وبدل الكتابة لا يفتقر وجوبه إلى التقوم؛ كذا في الهداية وفي غاية البيان: وهذا تناقض من صاحب الهداية في كلامه لأنه جعل التدبير هنا سبباً بعد الموت وجعله في باب التدبير سبباً في الحال، ومذهب علمائنا أن التدبير سبب في الحال بخلاف سائر التعليقات فإنها ليست بأسباب في الحال اهـ. وجوابه أن كلامه في سقوط التقوم لأم الولد، فحاصل كلامه أن سبب سقوط التقوم في أمل الولد ثابت في الحال، وسبب سقوطه في المدير متأخر إلى ما بعد الموت لأن الأصل أن ينعقد السبب فيه بعد الموت كسائر التعليقات وإنما قلنا بانعقاده سبباً للحال على خلاف القياس لضرورة هي أن تأخره إلى وجود الشرط كغيره من التعليقات يوجب بطلانه لأن ما بعد الموت زمان زوال أهلية التصرف فلا تتأخر سببية كلامه فيتقدر بقدر الضرورة فيظهر أثره في حرمة البيع خاصة لا في سقوط التقوم فتتأخر سببيته لسقوط التقوم إلى ما بعد الموت، وهذا هو محمل كلام المصنف فلا تناقض كما في فتح القدير.
His saying: 'And there is no value for an Umm Walad' meaning she has no value according to Abu Hanifa, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: she is assessable (mutaqawwima) for benefit through intercourse, hire, and service, and this is assessment, and the prohibition of sale does not nullify her assessment as in the mudabbar. Do you not see that the Umm Walad of a Christian, if she becomes Muslim, has to seek earnings and this is a sign of assessment, except that her value is one-third of the value of a slave as they said due to loss of sale, and the seeking of earnings after death unlike the mudabbar due to loss of the benefit of sale, but seeking earnings and use remain. According to Abu Hanifa, assessment is by possession (ihraz), and she is possessed for lineage not for assessment, and possession for assessment is subsidiary. Therefore she does not seek earnings for a creditor or heir unlike the mudabbar. This is because lineage is actual in her present state, which is the freedom established through the child as known in the prohibition of marriage affinity, except that its effect has not appeared in the right of ownership due to necessity of benefit, so causation (tasabbub) operates to drop assessment, while in the mudabbar the cause is contracted after death and prohibition of sale in it is for the realization of its purpose, so they differ. In the Umm Walad of a Christian, we ruled that she is required to give a kitabah upon him to prevent harm from both sides, and the kitabah payment does not require assessment for its obligation; thus in al-Hidayah. In Ghayat al-Bayan: This is a contradiction from the author of al-Hidayah because he made tadbir here a cause after death and made it in the chapter of tadbir a cause in the present, and the madhhab of our scholars is that tadbir is a cause in the present unlike other suspended acts. The answer is that his words are about the fall of assessment for the Umm Walad. The gist is that the cause of falling of assessment for the Umm Walad is established in the present, while the cause of its fall in the mudabbar is delayed to after death because the original is that the cause in it is contracted after death like other suspended acts. We only said it is a cause in the present contrary to analogy due to necessity, because delaying it to the existence of the condition like other suspended acts invalidates it, because after death is a time of disappearance of legal capacity, so the causality of his words cannot be delayed, so it is limited to the extent of necessity, so its effect appears in the prohibition of sale only, not in the fall of assessment, so its causality for the fall of assessment is delayed to after death. This is the interpretation of the author's words, so there is no contradiction as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal value (taqawwum) of an Umm Walad. According to Abu Hanifa, she has no value because she is 'possessed' for lineage (nasab) and not for assessment; possession for assessment is secondary. Therefore she cannot be required to seek earnings for a creditor or heir. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they say she is assessable because benefit can be derived from her through intercourse, hire, and service, and the prohibition of sale does not negate value, similar to a mudabbar (a slave whose emancipation is deferred). They cite the example of a Christian's Umm Walad who must seek earnings if she becomes Muslim, indicating value. Abu Hanifa responds that her possession is for lineage, not for assessment, so earnings are not sought for others. The text then resolves an apparent contradiction in al-Hidayah: there tadbir is considered a cause after death for the fall of assessment, while in the chapter of tadbir it is considered a cause in the present. The answer is that tadbir's effect on prohibition of sale is immediate, but its effect on the fall of assessment is delayed until after death, due to the principle that delayed causality is invalid after death. Thus no contradiction.

Key Terms
التقوم (al-taqawwum): The legal quality of being valuable (mutaqawwim) such that compensation is due for its destruction.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Possession or acquisition that establishes legal value; here, the Umm Walad is 'possessed' for lineage, not for assessment.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is deferred to after the master's death; used as comparison for assessment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why, according to Abu Hanifa, does an Umm Walad lack legal value (taqawwum)?
A) Because she cannot be sold or transferred.
B) Because she is possessed for lineage (nasab) and not for assessment.
C) Because she is always pregnant.
D) Because she becomes free upon the master's death.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon view is that an Umm Walad has no legal value because she is held for lineage, not for assessment.

Interpretation of 'Yawma'idh' in Conditional Manumission Oaths
Detailed exegesis of the phrase 'yawma'idh' in manumission oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of the phrase 'yawma'idh' in conditional manumission oaths and determine which slaves are freed based on the timing of acquisition.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'yawma'idh' means the time of the condition, so slaves acquired after the oath but before the condition are freed.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the linguistic intricacies of the phrase 'yawma'idh' (on that day) as used in oaths of conditional manumission. The commentator argues that 'yawma'idh' should be interpreted as referring to the time of the condition (e.g., entry) rather than a literal day, drawing on Quranic usage and common Arabic practice. Understanding this interpretation is crucial for determining which slaves are freed when such an oath is uttered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what action is being evaluated (the conditional manumission oath) and what the Hanafi ruling is (slaves owned at time of entry are freed).
The act is a ta'liq of manumission.
2
Locate the linguistic dalil
Find the argument about the meaning of 'yawma'idh'. The commentator uses Quranic verse and Arabic usage to show it means the time of the condition.
This is a dalil aqli (linguistic reasoning).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling: the phrase 'yawma'idh' in the Quran and common speech refers to the time of an event, not a literal day.
The tanwīn substitutes for the omitted clause.
4
Note the practical implications
Understand that this interpretation includes slaves acquired after the oath but before the condition is fulfilled.
Contrast with the alternative interpretation that would only include slaves owned at the time of the oath.
5
Apply the principle to related cases
Consider variations like 'every slave I buy is free' where the temporal attachment changes.
The text contrasts this with 'every slave I own on that day'.

حتى لو دخل ليلاً عتق ما في ملكه لأنه أضيف إلى فعل لا يمتد وهو الدخول وإن كان في اللفظ إنما أضيف إلى لفظ (إذ المضافة للدخول لكن معنى إذ غير ملاحظ وإلا كان المراد يوم وقت الدخول، وهو وإن كان يمكن على معنى يوم الوقت الذي فيه الدخول تقييداً لليوم به لكن إذا أريد به مطلق الوقت يصير المعنى وقت وقت الدخول ونحن نعلم مثله كثيراً في الاستعمال الفصيح كنحو ويومئذ يفرح المؤمنون بنصر الله ولا يلاحظ فيه شيء من فإن قال عنيت أحد الصنفين دون الآخر لم يدين يوم في القضاء لأنه نوى تخصيص العموم وأنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدق في القضاء ويصدق فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لأن الله تعالى مطلع على نيته وفي البدائع أيضاً: لو قال كل مملوك اشتريه نهو حر إن كلمت فلاناً أو إذا كلمت فلاناً أو إذا جاء الغد ولا نية له فهذا يقع على ما يشتريه يل الكلام، فكل مملوك اشتراه قبل الكلام ثم كلم عتق وما اشتراه بعد الكلام لا يعتق.
Chapter of swearing by entry. Thus in some copies, and the correct is Chapter of swearing by manumission as in al-Hidayah. Its meaning is that he makes manumission a recompense for the oath by conditionally linking manumission to something. This is the beginning of explaining conditionality after mentioning matters of immediate effectiveness. He only mentioned the issue of conditionality by birth in the chapter of partial manumission to clarify that part of it is freed when knowledge is lacking. The word 'hulf' (swearing) with a fatha on the ha and sukun on the lam, or kasra, is the source of their saying 'he swore by Allah, yahlifu, hulfan and hilfan' meaning oath. With a kasra on the ha and sukun on the lam, it means covenant. His statement (the author): 'Whoever says: If you enter, then every slave I own on that day is free, then what he owns after this statement is freed by entry' means after this statement by entry, because the tanwin in 'yawma'idh' is a substitute for the clause to which the word 'idh' is added, its implied meaning is 'when you enter'. The word 'day' is a time adverbial for the slave, so the implied meaning is: everyone who is in my ownership at the time of entry is free. This is in reality attributing the manumission of the slave on the day of entry to the entry, and a slave only exists through ownership, so it is as if he said: If I own a slave at the time of the day of entry, he is free. This applies to ownership before entry that continues to the time of entry, so it is as if he attributed manumission to ownership existing at the time of entry, unlike if he said to someone else's slave: If you enter the house, my slave is free, then he (the owner) buys him and he enters, it is not freed because he did not attribute manumission to his ownership explicitly or implicitly. The intended meaning of 'day' here is absolute time, so even if he enters at night, what he owns is freed because he attributed it to an action that does not extend (i.e. entry), even though in wording he attributed it to the word 'idh' which is added to entry. But the meaning of 'idh' is not considered; otherwise the intended meaning would be the day at the time of entry. And even though it is possible to mean the day that is the time of entry, restricting 'day' to it, but if absolute time is intended, the meaning becomes 'the time of the time of entry'. We know many such instances in eloquent usage, like 'And on that day, the believers will rejoice in Allah's victory' (Quran 30:4-5), where nothing of that is considered. It is not considered in this verse as the time they are defeated as the time of rejoicing, nor the day of defeat as the time of rejoicing. Many similar examples exist in the Book of Allah and elsewhere. Thus it is understood that the word 'idh' was only mentioned as a shortener as a substitute for the omitted clause, or as a support for it, meaning the tanwin since it is a single silent letter, for improvement; its meaning is not considered. Many such instances exist in the statements of Arabic scholars regarding some words, which is not hidden from one who has insight in it; as in Fath al-Qadir. And if the author had said 'what is owned by him at the time of entry' it would be more apparent, because what was in his ownership at the time of the oath and continued to the time of entry was not owned by him after the oath as a new ownership. In al-Badai': If he says 'Every slave I own today is free' without an intention, and he owns a slave, and he acquires another slave in that day, then what he owned and what he acquired are freed. Likewise if he said 'this month' or 'this year', because when he specified by day, month, or year, the specification must be beneficial. If it did not include except what was in his ownership at the time of the oath, it would not be beneficial. If he says 'I intended one of the two categories without the other', he is not believed in court, because he intended to specify the general and that is contrary to the apparent, so he is not believed in court but he is believed between himself and Allah, because Allah knows his intention. And in al-Badai' also: If he says 'Every slave I buy is free upon my speaking so-and-so' or 'if I speak so-and-so' or 'when tomorrow comes' without an intention, then this applies to what he buys before he speaks. So every slave he bought before speaking and then he speaks, he is freed; whatever he buys after speaking is not freed.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage explains the interpretation of the phrase 'every slave I own on that day' in oaths of conditional manumission. The author clarifies that 'yawma'idh' does not refer to a literal day but to the time of the condition (entry). He provides a linguistic analysis, drawing on Quranic usage, to show that 'idh' is often used as a substitute without its literal meaning. The key ruling: slaves owned at the time of entry are freed, including those acquired after the oath but before entry. However, if the master says 'every slave I buy', the condition attaches to purchases made before the speech. The text also addresses issues of intention and credibility in court.

Key Terms
يومئذ (yawma'idh): A phrase meaning 'on that day', but here interpreted as 'at the time of entry'.
حلف (half): Swearing an oath, often using manumission as a conditional penalty.
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional attachment of a legal effect (manumission) to a future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'If you enter, every slave I own on that day is free' and he acquires a slave after the oath but before entry, what happens?
A) The acquired slave is not freed because he was not owned at the time of the oath.
B) The acquired slave is freed because 'yawma'idh' refers to the time of entry.
C) Only the slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed.
D) The condition is invalid because it is too vague.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'yawma'idh' in manumission oaths refers to the time of the condition, so slaves acquired after the oath but before the condition are freed.

Can Emancipation Be a Dower? The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dower validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf on making emancipation a dower, explain the proof each side uses, and identify the operative (mufta bihi) ruling with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Emancipation is not property, so it cannot be a valid dower except as a special privilege for the Prophet.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute that tests the boundaries of what can constitute a dower (mahr). The core issue is whether the act of freeing a slave woman can itself be the dower for marrying her. The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that emancipation is not property and thus cannot fulfill the requirement of a dower, while Abu Yusuf permits it based on the Prophetic precedent of Safiyya. This lesson unpacks the proofs, the rebuttal, and the practical ruling that governs this scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
The passage describes a master who frees his slave woman on condition that she marries him, and she does. The act is valid marriage, but what is her dower? Identify that the dower is contested.
The marriage itself is sahih—the dispute is only about the dower.
2
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Two positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: emancipation is not mal (property), so it cannot be a dower; she gets mahr al-mithl. (2) Abu Yusuf: emancipation can be the dower because the Prophet did so with Safiyya.
This is not an inter-madhhab dispute but within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Analyze the Proofs
Abu Yusuf uses the hadith of Safiyya (dalil naqli). The majority responds by arguing that this was a special privilege (khususiyya) for the Prophet, not a general rule. The general principle is that dower must be property with financial value.
The majority's position aligns with the Hanafi usul rule that general commands are binding unless a specific exception is proven.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
If marriages occurs under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the woman receives mahr al-mithl; under Abu Yusuf, the emancipation itself suffices and no additional payment is due. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the majority view.
Also note: if she refuses marriage after emancipation, she must pay her value according to all three imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد المصنف باشتراط التزوج من الأجنبي لأنه لو أعتق أمته على أن تزوجه نفسها فزوجته نفسها كان لها مهر مثلها عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأن العتق ليس بمال فلا يصلح مهراً، وعند أبي يوسف يجوز جعل العتق صداقاً لأنه لا أعتق صفية ونكحها وجعل عتقها مهرها . قلنا : كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مخصوصاً بالنكاح بغير مهر فإن أبت أن تتزوجه فعليها قيمتها في قولهم جميعاً. وفي الخانية : أم الولد إذا أعتقها مولاها على أن تزوج نفسها منه فقبلت عتقت فإن أبت أن تزوج نفسها منه لا سعاية عليها والله أعلم.
And the author conditioned the requirement of marriage from an outsider, because if he freed his slave woman on condition that she marry him herself, and she married him, she would have a mahr of her like according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because emancipation is not property so it cannot be a valid dower. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible to make emancipation a dower because he freed Safiyya and married her and made her emancipation her mahr. We said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) was specified with marriage without a dower. If she refuses to marry him, then upon her is her value according to all of them. And in al-Khaniyya: If a master frees his umm walad on condition that she marry him herself, and she accepts, she becomes free. If she refuses to marry him, there is no service upon her. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a master who emancipates his slave woman on the condition that she marries him, and then the marriage actually takes place. The issue is whether the emancipation itself can serve as her dower (mahr). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, emancipation is not considered property (mal) and therefore cannot be a valid dower; instead, she is entitled to the mahr al-mithl (the appropriate dower for a woman of her status). Abu Yusuf holds that it is permissible to make emancipation a dower, citing the Prophetic precedent of marrying Safiyya and making her emancipation her mahr. The Hanafi response is that this was a special case for the Prophet alone. The passage also notes that if the slave woman refuses to marry after accepting emancipation, she must pay her value to the master according to all three imams. The ruling distinguishes between a regular slave woman and an umm walad (a slave mother of her master's child), where if she refuses to marry after emancipation, no service is required from her.

Key Terms
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The appropriate dower for a woman of similar status to the bride, used when a dower is not specified or is invalid.
صداق (sidaq): Synonym for mahr (dower), the payment due to a wife upon marriage.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master, thereby becoming free upon his death.
خصوصية (khususiyya): A special privilege or ruling specific to the Prophet (peace be upon him) that does not apply to the Ummah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The value of her emancipation
B) The mahr al-mithl appropriate for her
C) Nothing, because the marriage is invalid
D) A specified sum agreed upon by both

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, emancipation is not considered property and thus cannot serve as a dower; the majority view that mahr al-mithl is due remains the operative ruling.

Transactions with Umm al-Walad and Establishing Lineage
Rulings on permissibility with umm al-walad and lineage rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which transactions with an umm al-walad are permissible and explain the Hanafi rule that her child's lineage requires a claim from the master for the first child, while subsequent children are automatically attributed.

Core Idea
An umm al-walad remains fully owned, so most transactions are permissible; but her child's lineage requires a claim from the master, except subsequent children after acknowledgment.

The umm al-walad occupies a unique position in Hanafi law: she is a slave who will become free upon her master's death, yet during his lifetime she remains his property. This lesson examines what the master may do with her—intercourse, service, hiring, marriage—and how paternity of her children is determined. The key distinction is that the first child requires an explicit claim (da'wa) from the master to become an umm al-walad, while later children follow automatically unless a prohibition blocks it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permissible transactions
List the four actions permitted with an umm al-walad given at the start of the passage: intercourse, use as a servant, hiring out, and marriage. Note the basis: ownership remains, so any transaction that does not invalidate her eventual freedom is allowed.
The passage compares her to a mudabbara (slave promised freedom on master's death).
2
Understand the ownership implications
Earnings from her labor, compensation for injury (aqr), and her marriage dower belong to the master because he owns both the benefit and the object.
This follows from full ownership.
3
Analyze the marriage and istibra rule
The master is not required to observe an istibra (waiting period) before marrying her off; it is only recommended. Then examine the timing of birth to determine paternity: before six months → child from master; after six months → child from husband.
Timing is critical: less than six months indicates pregnancy before marriage.
4
Examine the condition of da'wa for becoming umm al-walad
A female slave becomes umm al-walad only if the master claims the child as his own. This is because intercourse with a slave is primarily for pleasure, not procreation, unlike marriage. Once the first child is claimed, subsequent children are automatically attributed without a new claim.
The claim can be explicit or implied by acknowledging the first child.
5
Identify exceptions where lineage is not automatic
If the slave becomes forbidden to the master (permanent or temporary prohibition), subsequent children require a fresh claim. Temporary prohibitions that do not remove the 'bed' (e.g., menstruation, ihram) do not affect automatic lineage.
The passage also discusses a case of three children and the effect of selective acknowledgment.

Source Text

مني يثبت نسب الولد منه غلاماً كان أو جارية ولو قال إن كان في بطنك ولد فهو مني إلى سنتين فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر يثبت النسب منه، وإن ولدت لأكثر ستة أشهر لا يثبت النسب والتوقيت باطل .اهـ. وأطلق في ثبوت ثبوته بقوله هو ولدي بناء على أن وطأه حينئذ لقصد الولد، وعلى هذا قال بعض فضلاء الدرس : ينبغي أنه إذا أقر أنه كان لا يعزل عنها وحصنها أن يثبت نسبه من غير توقف على دعواه، وإن كنا نوجب عليه في هذه الحالة الاعتراف به فلا حاجة أن نوجب عليه الاعتراف ليعترف فيثبت نسبه بل يثبت نسبه ابتداء، وأظن أن لا بعد في أن يحكم على المذهب بذلك اهـ. وأقول : إنه لا يصح
She may be subjected to intercourse, used as a servant, hired out, and married off, because ownership remains in her, so she resembles a mudabbara. Every disposition that invalidates this right is not permissible in her, and whatever does not invalidate it is permissible. He indicated by intercourse and usage that earnings, produce, dower, and bride-price are for the master, because they are in exchange for the benefit, and the benefits are in his ownership, and likewise the ownership of the object remains. He indicated by marriage that istibra (waiting period) is not obligatory; they said it is recommended like the istibra of the seller, due to the possibility that she may have become pregnant from him, thus the marriage would be invalid, so it is an exposure to invalidity. If he married her off and she gave birth before six months, the child is from the master and the marriage is invalid, because it becomes clear that he married her while she had in her womb a child whose paternity is established from him. If she gives birth after more than six months, the child is from the husband even if the master claims it, but the child becomes free due to the master's acknowledgment of his freedom, even if lineage is not established. In al-Muhit: If he sells her service to her or contracts kitabah upon her for her service, it is permissible, and she becomes free when he sells her service to her. The statement: 'If she gives birth after him, his lineage is established without claim, unlike the first' is an explanation of the condition for her becoming umm al-walad. He indicated that if a female slave gives birth, she does not become umm al-walad unless he claims the child as his own, because intercourse with a female slave is intended for fulfilling desire, not for children, due to the existence of a hindrance, so a claim is necessary, like ownership by right hand without intercourse, unlike marriage because the child is definitely intended there, so no need for claim. If he acknowledges the first child and she brings a second, his lineage is established without a claim from the master, because by claiming the first, the child is designated as intended from him, so the claim becomes, the lineage is established with her first, as they said: If he claims the lineage of the child of his female slave whom he married to his slave, his lineage is only established from the slave, not from the master, and she becomes umm al-walad for him by his acknowledgment of the establishment of lineage from him even if he does not confirm her. In al-Zahiriyyah: If he says to his slave girl, 'If what is in your womb is a boy, it is from me; if it is a girl, it is not from me,' the lineage of the child is established from him whether it is a boy or a girl. If he says, 'If there is a child in your womb, it is from me until two years,' and she gives birth before six months, lineage is established from him; if she gives birth after six months, lineage is not established, and the timing is invalid. He gave absolute ruling that the second's lineage is established without claim, but it is conditioned by that she is not forbidden to him, whether the prohibition is permanent or temporary. If she is forbidden to him, his lineage is not established except by claim, because the apparent is that he did not have intercourse with her after the prohibition, so the prohibition of intercourse is like a denial by implication, as if the master's son or father had intercourse with her, or the master had intercourse with her mother or daughter and she gave birth after more than six months, or he married her off and she gave birth at six months from the time of marriage. If he claims in permanent prohibition, lineage is established because prohibition does not remove ownership; in the married case, the child is emancipated. Similarly, if she is forbidden to him by kitabah. If she is forbidden to him by something that does not cut off the marriage of a free woman and does not remove her bed, like menstruation, postpartum bleeding, ihram, and fasting, then lineage is established without claim, because it is a temporary prohibition that does not change the ruling of the bed. This is found in al-Bada'i. And the apparent restriction to more than six months indicates that if she gives birth after the occurrence of the prohibition in less than six months, his lineage is established without claim due to certainty that the conception was before the prohibition; this has been mentioned in Fath al-Qadir as a discussion. In al-Zahiriyyah: A man's female slave gave birth in his ownership to three children in different pregnancies. If he claims the youngest, the lineage of the youngest is established from him, and he may sell the last two by agreement. If he claims the lineage of the eldest, the lineage of the eldest is established from him, and the middle and youngest are like the mother; their lineage is not established, and he may not sell them because it is incumbent upon him in law to acknowledge the lineage of a child who is from him. Since he specified the eldest with the claim after this became incumbent upon him in law, this constitutes a denial of the latter two. The child of an umm al-walad has his lineage negated by denial, and this is analogous to what is said: silence is not a proof, but silence after the necessity of explanation makes a proof of denial. So this is similar. And he restricted it to claim because if he said, 'I used to have intercourse intending the child at the time of her bringing the child,' lineage is not established because he did not acknowledge the child. In Fath al-Qadir: It should be that lineage is established without claim because its establishment by his saying 'he is my son' is based on the fact that his intercourse at that time was for the purpose of the child. Based on this, some learned teachers said: It should be that if he admits that he never used coitus interruptus with her and she was fortified, his lineage should be established without awaiting his claim. Although we oblige him in this case to acknowledge it, there is no need to oblige him to acknowledge in order that he acknowledges and his lineage is established; rather, his lineage is established ab initio. I think there is no harm in ruling according to the school on that. I say: It is not correct.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage covers the permissibility of various transactions with an umm al-walad and detailed rules for establishing paternity (lineage) of her children. The passage first states that an umm al-walad may be subjected to intercourse, used for service, hired out, and married off, because the master's full ownership remains in her, similar to a mudabbara (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death). Any transaction that would invalidate the right of umm al-walad (i.e., her eventual freedom upon the master's death) is not permissible; others are permissible. Earnings from her labor, any compensation for injury (aqr), and the marriage dower (mahr) belong to the master. Regarding marriage, the master is not required to observe an istibra (waiting period to ensure no pregnancy) before marrying her off, though it is recommended. If the master marries her off and she gives birth before six months, the child is from the master and the marriage is invalid because she was already pregnant from the master. If she gives birth after six months, the child is from the husband, unless the master claims it, in which case the child is freed due to the master's acknowledgment but lineage is not established. The passage then discusses the key condition for a female slave to become an umm al-walad: the master must claim the child as his own. This claim (da'wa) is necessary because intercourse with a slave is primarily for pleasure, unlike marriage where procreation is intended. Once the master claims the first child, subsequent children from the same slave automatically have their lineage established without a new claim. However, this automatic establishment is conditioned on the slave not being forbidden to the master due to a permanent or temporary prohibition. Permanent prohibitions (e.g., consanguinity, fosterage) require a separate claim to establish lineage; temporary prohibitions that do not remove the bed (like menstruation, ihram, fasting) do not prevent automatic lineage. The passage also covers a case of three children born in separate pregnancies: if the master claims only the youngest, the lineage of the youngest is established and he may sell the older two; if he claims the oldest, only the oldest's lineage is established and he may not sell the younger two because acknowledging the oldest implies that the others are not from him, which amounts to a denial. Finally, the passage presents a discussion from Fath al-Qadir indicating that if the master explicitly states he intended offspring (not just pleasure) or admits to never using coitus interruptus, lineage might be established without a formal claim, though the author of the sharh (likely Ibn Nujaym) rejects that view, stating it is not correct.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who bears a child from her master that he acknowledges as his own, and thereby becomes free upon his death.
الدعوة (al-da'wah): The master's verbal claim (da'wa) that the child is his own, which is required to establish paternity for a slave child.
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): A waiting period to ensure the womb is clear of pregnancy before a transaction (like marriage) involving a female slave.
المدبرة (al-mudabbara): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death, used here as an analogy for umm al-walad regarding remaining ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master has an umm al-walad who gives birth to a child. He claims the child. She then gives birth to a second child. Under which condition would the second child's lineage NOT be automatically established?
A) If the master had intended offspring from the beginning.
B) If the master is in a state of ihram.
C) If the umm al-walad becomes forbidden to the master due to a permanent prohibition, like his son having intercourse with her.
D) If the second child is born less than six months after a temporary prohibition.

Key Takeaway
An umm al-walad may be used, hired, and married off, but her first child requires a claim; subsequent children are automatic unless a prohibition blocks it.

Umm Walad by Purchase After Birth in Nikah
Ruling with proof via talil (juz'iyyah and impediment removal)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman becoming umm walad through purchase after giving birth in a valid marriage and explain the legal reasoning (juz'iyyah and removal of impediment).

Core Idea
A child's lineage through valid marriage establishes juz'iyyah, and purchase after birth removes the only impediment to umm walad status.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario from Hanafi fiqh: a woman bears a child during a valid marriage, but at that time she is owned by someone else. Later, the father purchases her. Does she become his umm walad? The passage explains the underlying cause (sabab) and the removal of the impediment, providing a clear illustration of talil (legal reasoning) in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that if a woman gives birth during a valid marriage and then the father (the child's owner) purchases her, she becomes his umm walad. The ruling is that the status of umm walad is established.
Umm walad means the mother of the child, who becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.
2
Identify the cause (sabab): juz'iyyah
The cause for umm walad status is 'juz'iyyah' — the partiality or legal connection between master and slave through the child. Since the child is part of each parent, juz'iyyah exists.
Juz'iyyah is established when a single child is related completely to both the man and the woman.
3
Identify the impediment at the time of birth
At the moment of birth, the woman was owned by someone else (the original husband or a third party?). This ownership by another prevented juz'iyyah from taking effect immediately.
The impediment (mani') is that the woman was not owned by the father at birth.
4
Show the removal of the impediment by purchase
When the father later purchases her, her ownership transfers to him. The impediment of another's ownership is removed, allowing juz'iyyah to establish umm walad status.
The purchase is the means of removal; the status is retroactive in effect but not in time.
5
Confirm the condition: valid marriage
The passage emphasizes that the birth must occur in a valid marriage (nikah), not from zina or an invalid marriage. Lineage only follows from valid marriage.
If the birth is from zina, lineage is not established, and juz'iyyah is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو ولدت بنكاح فملكها فهي أم ولده لأن السبب هو الجزئية على ما ذكرنا من قبل والجزئية إنما تثبت بينهما بنسبة الولد الواحد إلى كل منهما كملاً وقد ثبت النسب فتثبت الجزئية بهذه الواسطة، وقد كان المانع حين الولادة ملك الغير وقد زال قيد بالنكاح الحنفي فليس له الحكم به الخ) قال في المنح : يمكن أن يراد به الحنفي ويكون من باب قضائه بخلاف رأيه وفيه الخلاف بين أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه .
His statement: 'If she gives birth during a marriage and then he owns her, she is his umm walad because the cause is partiality (juz'iyyah) as we mentioned before, and partiality is only established between them by the relation of the single child to each of them completely, and lineage has been established, so partiality is established through this intermediary. The impediment at the time of birth was the ownership of someone else, and it has been removed.' Restriction by marriage. The Hanafi judge is not given authority to rule based on his own opinion etc. He said in Al-Minh: It can be meant by the Hanafi, and it is from the category of his ruling contrary to his own view, and in it there is a difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a commentary statement clarifying the ruling from the matn: if a woman gives birth to a child during a valid marriage (nikah) and then the father later purchases her, she becomes his umm walad. The reasoning (talil) is that the cause of being umm walad is the partiality (juz'iyyah) between master and slave: the child relates partially to each. Since the child's lineage (nasab) is established through the valid marriage, the condition of partiality is fulfilled. The only impediment at the time of birth was that the mother was owned by someone else (the original husband?), but that ownership has now been removed through purchase. So the impediment is gone. The phrase 'qayyada bi'l-nikah' (restricted by marriage) indicates that this ruling applies specifically to a child born in a valid marriage, not to a child from zina. The commentator then notes a potential issue: the judge in this case is Hanafi but may have to rule contrary to his own school view (qadahu bi-khilaf ra'yihi) because the evidence supports the opposite? Actually the text is ambiguous. The reference to Al-Minh suggests it may refer to a Hanafi judge ruling on a case according to another school. The passage ends by noting ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two companions on some aspect. Further detail from the original page context suggests that this ikhtilaf concerns whether the judge can rule based on his personal opinion. The overall ruling is clear: upon purchase, the woman becomes umm walad and the child is free.

Key Terms
الجزئية (al-juz'iyyah): The legal connection between master and slave that makes the child part of each; partiality.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership. Here, the obstacle at birth was that the mother was owned by another; once she is owned by the father, the obstacle is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a woman become umm walad for a man who purchases her after she bore his child during a valid marriage?
A) Because the child's lineage established partiality, and the impediment of another's ownership is removed.
B) Because she was already his wife.
C) Because he owned her at the time of birth.
D) Because she consented.

Key Takeaway
A woman becomes umm walad upon purchase after birth in a valid marriage because juz'iyyah is established through lineage and the impediment of another's ownership is removed.

Claiming a Child from a Jointly Owned Slave-Girl: The Core Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with talil reasoning on istilad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one co-owner claims a child from a jointly owned slave-girl and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether istilad is indivisible.

Core Idea
Lineage and istilad are indivisible; the claimant pays half value and half dower, not full value.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when one co-owner of a slave-girl claims paternity of her child, the child's lineage is established, the slave-girl becomes his umm walad, and he must compensate his partner. The central legal issue is whether the status of umm walad applies to the entire slave-girl or only to the claimant's share. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the ruling and the difference between Abu Hanifa and his two students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its components
From the text, list the four effects of the claim: lineage established, slave-girl becomes umm walad, half value due, half dower due.
No full value or child value due.
2
Trace the reasoning for lineage indivisibility
The text argues that lineage is established entirely because its cause (conception) is indivisible. Why can't the child be partly from one father and partly from another?
This is a universal principle in fiqh: lineage cannot be split.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on istilad indivisibility
Abu Hanifa holds that istilad (causing a slave-girl to bear a child) is indivisible, so the entire slave-girl becomes umm walad immediately. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that only the claimant's share becomes umm walad, and he must then acquire the partner's share.
The phrase 'عنده' refers to Abu Hanifa, 'عندهما' to the Sahibayn.
4
Understand the justification for half value and half dower
Half value compensates for acquiring the partner's share. Half dower compensates for intercourse with the partner's share, since the intercourse is not zina due to partial ownership.
Compare with the case of a father and son: if the father has no ownership, no dower is due because ownership must exist before intercourse.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The matn's opening statement reflects Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position. The Sahibayn's view is acknowledged but not adopted for fatwa.
This is an example of tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو ادعى ولد أمة مشتركة ثبت نسبه وهي أم ولده ولزمه نصف قيمتها ونصف عقرها لا قيمته) أما ثبوت النسب فلأنه لما ثبت في نصفه لمصادفته ملكه ثبت في الباقي ضرورة أنه لا يتجزأ لما أن سببه لا يتجزأ وهو العلوق إذ الولد الواحد لا يعلق من مائين، وأما صيرورتها أم ولد فلأن الاستيلاد لا يتجزأ عنده وعندهما يصير نصيبه أم ولد له ثم يتملك نصيب صاحبه إذ هو قابل للملك. وأما ضمان نصف القيمة فلأنه تملك نصيب صاحبه لما استكمل الاستيلاد، وأما ضمان نصف العقر فلأنه وطئ جارية مشتركة إذ الملك ثبت حكماً للاستيلاد فيعقبه الملك في نصيب صاحبه بخلاف الأب إذ استولد جارية ابنه لأن الملك هناك ثبت شرطاً للاستيلاد فيتقدمه فصار واطئاً ملك نفسه. وأما الولد فلأن النسب يثبت مستنداً إلى وقت العلوق فلم يتعلق شيء منه على ملك شريكه. أطلق في المدعي فشمل الحر والمكاتب فإذا ادعى المكاتب ولد الأمة المشتركة فالحكم كذلك كما في البدائع وفي الظهيرية: وإن كانت بين حر ومكاتب فادعى المكاتب وحده ثبت نسبه وضمن نصف قيمتها للشريك. وقال أبو يوسف: نصيب الشريك بحاله كما كان يستخدمها كل واحد منهما يوماً فإذا عجز المكاتب كان له أن يبيعها لأن حكم الاستيلاد في نصيب المكاتب بصفة الاستقرار لم يثبت بدليل أنها تباع بعد العجز. اهـ. ومثل المسلم الكافر والصحيح والمريض مرض الموت لأنه من الحوائج الأصلية. وأطلق في الأمة فشمل ما إذا كانت حبلت على ملكهما أو اشترياها حاملاً لكنه يضمن في الثاني نصف قيمة الولد لأنها دعوة إعتاق لا استيلاد. وفي الظهيرية: لو اشترى أخوان أمة حاملة فجاءت بولد فادعاه أحدهما فعليه نصف قيمة الولد لأنه أعتقه بالدعوة ولا يعتق على عمه بالقرابة لأن الدعوة قد تقدمت فيضاف الحكم إلى الدعوة دون القرابة. اهـ. وأطلق في وجوب نصف القيمة والعقر فشمل الموسر والمعسر لأنه ضمان تملك بخلاف ضمان العتق وتعتبر القيمة يوم العلوق وكذا نصف العقر. وشمل ما إذا كان المدعي منهما الأب كما إذا كان مشتركة بين الأب وابنه فادعاه الأب صح ولزمه نصف القيمة والعقر كالأجنبي بخلاف ما إذا استولدها ولا ملك له فيها حيث لا يجب العقر عندنا. والفرق بينهما أن الجارية متى لم تكن ملكاً له مست الحاجة إلى إثبات الملك له فيها سابقاً على الوطء لئلا يكون فعله زنا، ومتى كانت مشتركة بينهما فقيام الملك في شقص منها يكفي لإخراج فعله عن أن يكون زنا فلم تمس الحاجة إلى إثبات الملك له سابقاً على الوطء فلذا يجب نصف العقر؛ كذا في الظهيرية.
His statement: (If he claims a child from a jointly owned slave-girl, his lineage is established, she becomes his umm walad, and he must pay half her value and half her dower, not her full value.) As for the establishment of lineage, because when it is established in his half due to its coinciding with his ownership, it is established in the remainder necessarily because it is indivisible, since its cause is indivisible: conception, for a single child cannot be conceived from two males. As for her becoming umm walad, because istilad is indivisible according to him (Abu Hanifa), while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his share becomes umm walad for him, then he acquires ownership of his partner's share since it is subject to ownership. As for the guarantee of half the value, because he acquires ownership of his partner's share upon completion of istilad. As for the guarantee of half the dower, because he had intercourse with a jointly owned slave-girl, since ownership is established legally for istilad so it is followed by ownership of the partner's share, unlike the father who causes a slave-girl of his son to bear a child, because there ownership is established as a condition for istilad and thus precedes it, so he becomes one who has intercourse with his own property. As for the child, lineage is established traced back to the time of conception, so nothing of it is attached to the partner's ownership. He made the claimant general, including the free man and the mukatab. If the mukatab claims a child from a jointly owned slave-girl, the ruling is likewise, as in al-Badai'. In al-Zahiriyya: If the slave-girl is between a free man and a mukatab, and the mukatab alone claims, his lineage is established and he guarantees half her value to the partner. Abu Yusuf said: The partner's share remains as it was, each using her on alternate days; if the mukatab becomes unable, he may sell her, because the ruling of istilad in the mukatab's share has not been established with stability, as evidenced by her being sold after his inability. End quote. The Muslim and the disbeliever, the healthy and the one terminally ill are alike, because it is from primary needs. He made the slave-girl general, including if she was conceived in their joint ownership or if they bought her pregnant. But in the second case he guarantees half the value of the child, because it is a claim of emancipation, not istilad. In al-Zahiriyya: If two brothers buy a pregnant slave-girl and she gives birth, and one claims the child, he must pay half the value of the child because he emancipated it by the claim, and it does not become free on his uncle by kinship, because the claim preceded, so the ruling is attributed to the claim, not the kinship. End quote. He made the obligation of half the value and dower general, including the wealthy and the insolvent, because it is a guarantee of acquisition unlike the guarantee of emancipation. The value is assessed at the time of conception, and likewise half the dower. It includes the case where the claimant is the father, such as if the slave-girl is jointly owned between the father and his son, and the father claims; it is valid and he must pay half the value and dower like a stranger, unlike if he causes her to bear a child when he has no ownership in her, where the dower is not obligatory according to us. The difference between them is that when the slave-girl is not his property, there is need to establish ownership for him prior to intercourse to prevent his act from being zina, whereas when she is jointly owned between them, the existence of ownership in a share is sufficient to remove his act from being zina, so there is no need to establish ownership for him prior to intercourse, thus half the dower is obligatory; as in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the claim of paternity (istilad) by one co-owner of a jointly owned slave-girl. The author explains the ruling of the matn: if one co-owner claims the child, the child's lineage is established to him, the slave-girl becomes his umm walad, and he must pay half her value and half her dower (not full value). The reasoning: lineage is indivisible, so when it is established in his share (because the slave-girl is partly his), it necessarily extends to the entire child. Istilad (causing a slave-girl to bear a child) is also indivisible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, so she immediately becomes his umm walad entirely. According to the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), only his share becomes umm walad, and then he must acquire the partner's share by paying half the value. The half dower (mahr) is due because intercourse with a jointly owned slave-girl is not outright zina (since part is his), but he still needs to compensate the partner for the use of her share. The passage then discusses various cases: a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) claiming, the condition of the joint owner, the case where the slave-girl was bought pregnant (then it's a claim of emancipation, not istilad, so half the child's value is due), and the case of a father claiming from a child jointly owned with his son. Throughout, the text follows the order of the matn and provides detailed legal reasoning (ta'lil) from the Hanafi perspective.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who bears a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death and cannot be sold.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master causing his slave-girl to bear a child, whether by intercourse or by claiming paternity.
عقر (ʿaqr): The dower (mahr) paid for intercourse with a slave-girl, akin to the dower of a free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the claimant not have to pay the full value of the slave-girl to his partner?
A) Because the claimant already owns half.
B) Because the partner is compensated by the dower.
C) Because the claimant only acquires the partner's share through istilad.
D) Because the child's lineage is enough compensation.

Key Takeaway
When a co-owner claims paternity, lineage and istilad are indivisible, so the slave-girl becomes his umm walad entirely and he compensates his partner with half value and half dower.

Special Cases in Claiming a Child from a Jointly Owned Slave-Girl
Extensions and special cases within the same legal framework

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling on claiming a child from a jointly owned slave-girl to special cases involving a mukatab, a pregnant slave-girl, and a father co-owner.

Core Idea
The ruling extends to mukatab, Muslim/kafir, healthy/ill; claiming a bought pregnant slave-girl is emancipation, not istilad; father pays like a stranger.

The previous lesson covered the core ruling and the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. This lesson explores the extensions of that ruling to various scenarios: a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), a slave-girl bought while pregnant, and a father who co-owns with his son. Each case introduces a nuance: in the mukatab case, Abu Yusuf holds that the partner's share can be sold if the mukatab becomes unable; in the pregnant case, the claim is treated as emancipation, not istilad; in the father's case, the ruling is similar to that of a stranger, unlike the case where the father has no ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special cases discussed in the text
The text mentions: claiming by a mukatab, claiming a child from a pregnant slave-girl bought jointly, and claiming by a father who co-owns with his son.
These are extensions of the core ruling.
2
Analyze the mukatab case and Abu Yusuf's objection
The text applies the ruling to a mukatab. But Abu Yusuf notes that if the mukatab becomes unable to pay, his claim may not establish permanent umm walad status, and the partner may sell his share. This shows a qualification based on the mukatab's precarious ownership.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority within the school.
3
Understand the pregnant slave-girl case
If two co-owners buy a pregnant slave-girl, and one claims the child, it is a claim of emancipation, not istilad. The child is already conceived before their ownership, so claiming it does not make her umm walad. He must pay half the child's value to the partner because he has emancipated the child by claiming it.
This is a distinction between claiming a child conceived during joint ownership vs. before.
4
Examine the father-son co-ownership case
If a father and son jointly own a slave-girl, and the father claims the child, the ruling is the same as for a stranger: he pays half value and half dower. This is contrasted with the case where the father has no ownership (e.g., he causes his son's slave-girl to bear a child), where no dower is due because the intercourse would be zina without prior ownership.
The difference is whether ownership exists at the time of intercourse.
5
Synthesize the underlying principles
The text shows that the core ruling applies unless there is a special circumstance that changes the nature of the claim: precarious ownership (mukatab), pre-conceived child (pregnant purchase), or the need to avoid zina (father without ownership).
These are examples of how fiqh principles are applied to nuanced cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو ادعى ولد أمة مشتركة ثبت نسبه وهي أم ولده ولزمه نصف قيمتها ونصف عقرها لا قيمته) أما ثبوت النسب فلأنه لما ثبت في نصفه لمصادفته ملكه ثبت في الباقي ضرورة أنه لا يتجزأ لما أن سببه لا يتجزأ وهو العلوق إذ الولد الواحد لا يعلق من مائين، وأما صيرورتها أم ولد فلأن الاستيلاد لا يتجزأ عنده وعندهما يصير نصيبه أم ولد له ثم يتملك نصيب صاحبه إذ هو قابل للملك. وأما ضمان نصف القيمة فلأنه تملك نصيب صاحبه لما استكمل الاستيلاد، وأما ضمان نصف العقر فلأنه وطئ جارية مشتركة إذ الملك ثبت حكماً للاستيلاد فيعقبه الملك في نصيب صاحبه بخلاف الأب إذ استولد جارية ابنه لأن الملك هناك ثبت شرطاً للاستيلاد فيتقدمه فصار واطئاً ملك نفسه. وأما الولد فلأن النسب يثبت مستنداً إلى وقت العلوق فلم يتعلق شيء منه على ملك شريكه. أطلق في المدعي فشمل الحر والمكاتب فإذا ادعى المكاتب ولد الأمة المشتركة فالحكم كذلك كما في البدائع وفي الظهيرية: وإن كانت بين حر ومكاتب فادعى المكاتب وحده ثبت نسبه وضمن نصف قيمتها للشريك. وقال أبو يوسف: نصيب الشريك بحاله كما كان يستخدمها كل واحد منهما يوماً فإذا عجز المكاتب كان له أن يبيعها لأن حكم الاستيلاد في نصيب المكاتب بصفة الاستقرار لم يثبت بدليل أنها تباع بعد العجز. اهـ. ومثل المسلم الكافر والصحيح والمريض مرض الموت لأنه من الحوائج الأصلية. وأطلق في الأمة فشمل ما إذا كانت حبلت على ملكهما أو اشترياها حاملاً لكنه يضمن في الثاني نصف قيمة الولد لأنها دعوة إعتاق لا استيلاد. وفي الظهيرية: لو اشترى أخوان أمة حاملة فجاءت بولد فادعاه أحدهما فعليه نصف قيمة الولد لأنه أعتقه بالدعوة ولا يعتق على عمه بالقرابة لأن الدعوة قد تقدمت فيضاف الحكم إلى الدعوة دون القرابة. اهـ. وأطلق في وجوب نصف القيمة والعقر فشمل الموسر والمعسر لأنه ضمان تملك بخلاف ضمان العتق وتعتبر القيمة يوم العلوق وكذا نصف العقر. وشمل ما إذا كان المدعي منهما الأب كما إذا كان مشتركة بين الأب وابنه فادعاه الأب صح ولزمه نصف القيمة والعقر كالأجنبي بخلاف ما إذا استولدها ولا ملك له فيها حيث لا يجب العقر عندنا. والفرق بينهما أن الجارية متى لم تكن ملكاً له مست الحاجة إلى إثبات الملك له فيها سابقاً على الوطء لئلا يكون فعله زنا، ومتى كانت مشتركة بينهما فقيام الملك في شقص منها يكفي لإخراج فعله عن أن يكون زنا فلم تمس الحاجة إلى إثبات الملك له سابقاً على الوطء فلذا يجب نصف العقر؛ كذا في الظهيرية.
His statement: (If he claims a child from a jointly owned slave-girl, his lineage is established, she becomes his umm walad, and he must pay half her value and half her dower, not her full value.) As for the establishment of lineage, because when it is established in his half due to its coinciding with his ownership, it is established in the remainder necessarily because it is indivisible, since its cause is indivisible: conception, for a single child cannot be conceived from two males. As for her becoming umm walad, because istilad is indivisible according to him (Abu Hanifa), while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his share becomes umm walad for him, then he acquires ownership of his partner's share since it is subject to ownership. As for the guarantee of half the value, because he acquires ownership of his partner's share upon completion of istilad. As for the guarantee of half the dower, because he had intercourse with a jointly owned slave-girl, since ownership is established legally for istilad so it is followed by ownership of the partner's share, unlike the father who causes a slave-girl of his son to bear a child, because there ownership is established as a condition for istilad and thus precedes it, so he becomes one who has intercourse with his own property. As for the child, lineage is established traced back to the time of conception, so nothing of it is attached to the partner's ownership. He made the claimant general, including the free man and the mukatab. If the mukatab claims a child from a jointly owned slave-girl, the ruling is likewise, as in al-Badai'. In al-Zahiriyya: If the slave-girl is between a free man and a mukatab, and the mukatab alone claims, his lineage is established and he guarantees half her value to the partner. Abu Yusuf said: The partner's share remains as it was, each using her on alternate days; if the mukatab becomes unable, he may sell her, because the ruling of istilad in the mukatab's share has not been established with stability, as evidenced by her being sold after his inability. End quote. The Muslim and the disbeliever, the healthy and the one terminally ill are alike, because it is from primary needs. He made the slave-girl general, including if she was conceived in their joint ownership or if they bought her pregnant. But in the second case he guarantees half the value of the child, because it is a claim of emancipation, not istilad. In al-Zahiriyya: If two brothers buy a pregnant slave-girl and she gives birth, and one claims the child, he must pay half the value of the child because he emancipated it by the claim, and it does not become free on his uncle by kinship, because the claim preceded, so the ruling is attributed to the claim, not the kinship. End quote. He made the obligation of half the value and dower general, including the wealthy and the insolvent, because it is a guarantee of acquisition unlike the guarantee of emancipation. The value is assessed at the time of conception, and likewise half the dower. It includes the case where the claimant is the father, such as if the slave-girl is jointly owned between the father and his son, and the father claims; it is valid and he must pay half the value and dower like a stranger, unlike if he causes her to bear a child when he has no ownership in her, where the dower is not obligatory according to us. The difference between them is that when the slave-girl is not his property, there is need to establish ownership for him prior to intercourse to prevent his act from being zina, whereas when she is jointly owned between them, the existence of ownership in a share is sufficient to remove his act from being zina, so there is no need to establish ownership for him prior to intercourse, thus half the dower is obligatory; as in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the claim of paternity (istilad) by one co-owner of a jointly owned slave-girl. The author explains the ruling of the matn: if one co-owner claims the child, the child's lineage is established to him, the slave-girl becomes his umm walad, and he must pay half her value and half her dower (not full value). The reasoning: lineage is indivisible, so when it is established in his share (because the slave-girl is partly his), it necessarily extends to the entire child. Istilad (causing a slave-girl to bear a child) is also indivisible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, so she immediately becomes his umm walad entirely. According to the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), only his share becomes umm walad, and then he must acquire the partner's share by paying half the value. The half dower (mahr) is due because intercourse with a jointly owned slave-girl is not outright zina (since part is his), but he still needs to compensate the partner for the use of her share. The passage then discusses various cases: a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) claiming, the condition of the joint owner, the case where the slave-girl was bought pregnant (then it's a claim of emancipation, not istilad, so half the child's value is due), and the case of a father claiming from a child jointly owned with his son. Throughout, the text follows the order of the matn and provides detailed legal reasoning (ta'lil) from the Hanafi perspective.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, paying installments for his freedom.
دعوة إعتاق (da'wat i'taq): A claim that results in the emancipation of the claimed person, as when claiming a child from a purchased pregnant slave-girl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling when two brothers buy a pregnant slave-girl and one claims the child?
A) The claimant must pay half the value of the mother.
B) The claimant must pay half the value of the child.
C) The claimant must pay half the dower.
D) The claimant does not have to pay anything.

Key Takeaway
Special cases modify the core ruling: for a pregnant slave-girl, it's emancipation; for a father, it's like a stranger; for a mukatab, Abu Yusuf allows sale of partner's share on default.

Priority Among Claimants of a Child from a Joint Slavewoman
Priority ranking of claimants for paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of priority among claimants of a child from a joint slavewoman based on their status (free Muslim, dhimmi, mukatab, mudabbir, slave) and explain the reasoning for each priority.

Core Idea
The free Muslim claimant has the strongest right to paternity because he combines Islam, freedom, and ownership.

This passage from al-Mabsut outlines the Hanafi ruling on multiple claimants of a child born to a jointly owned slavewoman. The priority is based on the status of each claimant: being Muslim, free, and having full ownership. Understanding this priority is crucial for determining lineage in mixed ownership scenarios, as it affects the child's status and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify all possible claimants
List the parties: free Muslim, dhimmi (free non-Muslim), mukatab (slave with kitaba contract), mudabbir (slave promised freedom at master's death), and ordinary slave.
The passage assumes each claimant asserts paternity.
2
Establish the order of priority
The free Muslim is first. If no Muslim, then the free dhimmi. Then the mukatab. Mudabbir and slave cannot establish paternity at all.
Priority is not a simple tie; it is a hierarchy.
3
Understand the rationale for each rank
Free Muslim combines Islam, freedom, and ownership. Dhimmi is free but not Muslim. Mukatab has a right to eventual freedom and ownership. Mudabbir and slave lack ownership entirely.
Ownership or its semblance is key.
4
Apply the claim vs. emancipation distinction
If one claimant claims the child and another emancipates the child, the claim takes precedence because it relates back to the moment of conception, while emancipation is only present.
Claim has retroactive effect; emancipation does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المبسوط : أمة بين مسلم وذمي ومكاتب ومدبر وعبد ولدت فادعوه فالحر المسلم أولى لاجتماع الإسلام والحرية فيه مع الملك، فإن لم يكن فيه مسلم بل من بعده فقط فالذمي أولى لأنه حر، والمكاتب والعبد وإن كانا مسلمين لكن نيل الولد تحصيل الإسلام دون الحرية ثم المكاتب لأن له حق ملك والولد على شرف الحرية بأداء الكتابة، وإن لم يكن مكاتب وادعى المدبر والعبد لا يثبت من واحد منهما النسب لأنهم ليس لهم ملك ولا شبهة ملك . قيل وجب أن يكون هذا الجواب في العبد المحجور وهبت له أمة ولا يتعين ذلك أن بين يزوج منها أيضاً؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي الظهيرية: ولو كانت الجارية بين رجل وأبيه وجده فجاءت بولد فادعوه كلهم فالجد أولى اهـ. وقيد بكون كل واحد منهما ادعى نسبه لأنها لو كانت بين رجلين فولدت ولداً فادعاه أحدهما وأعتقه الآخر وخرج الكلامان معاً كانت الدعوة أولى من الإعتاق لأن الدعوة تستند إلى حالة العلوق والإعتاق فيقتصر على الحال اهـ.
And in al-Mabsut: A slavewoman between a Muslim, a dhimmi, a mukatab, a mudabbir, and a slave gives birth, and they all claim the child. The free Muslim is most entitled because of the combination of Islam and freedom with ownership. If there is no Muslim, but only after him, then the dhimmi is most entitled because he is free. The mukatab and the slave, even if they are Muslim, the child attains Islam but not freedom. Then the mukatab, because he has a right of ownership and the child is on the verge of freedom through payment of kitaba. If there is no mukatab, and the mudabbir and the slave claim, lineage is not established from either because they have no ownership or semblance of ownership. It was said that this answer applies to a restricted slave who was given a slavewoman as a gift, but it is not restricted that he may also marry her? This is in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Zahiriyya: If the slavewoman is between a man, his father, and his grandfather, and she bears a child, and all claim him, then the grandfather is most entitled. It is conditioned that each one claims lineage because if she is between two men and she bears a child, and one claims him and the other emancipates him, and both statements come together, the claim takes precedence over emancipation because the claim relates to the time of conception, while emancipation is restricted to the present.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage from al-Mabsut discusses priority among multiple claimants (from a joint slavewoman) with different statuses. The free Muslim claimant wins because he has both Islam and freedom plus ownership. Among non-Muslims, the free dhimmi is preferred. A mukatab (who has a contract of kitaba) is preferred over a slave or mudabbir because he has a right to ownership and the child is on track to freedom. A mudabbir or slave cannot establish lineage because they lack full ownership. The condition of claiming is important: if one claims and the other emancipates, the claim is stronger because it retroacts to conception. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on the moment of conception for lineage.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave who has been promised freedom upon his master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is most entitled to claim paternity of a child born to a jointly owned slavewoman among a Muslim, a dhimmi, a mukatab, a mudabbir, and a slave?
A) The dhimmi because he is free.
B) The mukatab because he is near freedom.
C) The free Muslim because he has Islam and freedom.
D) The mudabbir because he will be free at death.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes the free Muslim claimant for paternity of a child from a joint slavewoman, followed by the free dhimmi, then the mukatab; mudabbir and slave cannot establish lineage due to lack of ownership.
