Unequal Shares in Jointly-Owned Slaves: Lineage and Liability
Rulings on joint paternity and financial liability with unequal shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a jointly-claimed child's lineage is equal regardless of ownership shares, and distinguish between cases where financial liability is equal (inheritance, child's tort) versus proportional (mother's tort).

Core Idea
Lineage is equal irrespective of ownership shares; financial liability may be equal or proportional depending on the source.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a subtle distinction in Hanafi fiqh: when two co-owners of a slavewoman with unequal shares both claim her child, the child is declared the son of both equally in terms of lineage. However, the financial consequences—inheritance from the child, blood money for his own tort, and compensation for the mother's tort—are not all treated alike. Understanding which rules follow proportionate ownership and which override it is key to mastering joint ownership cases in classical Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage covers (a) a jointly-owned slavewoman bearing a child claimed by both, and (b) a jointly-purchased male slave of unknown lineage claimed by both. In each, the shares are unequal (e.g., 1/10 and 9/10).
2
State the ruling on lineage
In both cases, the child is the son of both equally. No preference is given to the larger owner. Lineage is established without proportion.
This is a rule of nasab, not financial distribution.
3
Apply inheritance rules if the child dies
The two co-owners inherit from the child equally (half each), despite their unequal shares in the mother. This shows inheritance follows the status of fatherhood, not ownership.
4
Apply rules for the child's own tort (jinaayah)
If the child (now free) commits a tort, his 'aqila—which includes both co-owners—pay half each. Liability is equal.
The 'aqila here are the male agnates; the owners are the 'asaba of the child.
5
Apply rules for the mother's tort
If the slavewoman commits a tort, her owners pay proportionally to their ownership shares. The same proportional rule applies to her children (likely regarding their liability for her tort or for their own torts? The text states 'and likewise her children for them on this basis').
The text is ambiguous on the children's liability; we follow the explicit proportional rule for the mother.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في كونها مشتركة بينهما ولم يقيد باستوائهما في القدر لأنها لو كانت بين اثنين لأحدهما عشرها وللآخر تسعة أعشارها فجاءت بولد فادعياه معاً فإنه ابنهما، ابن هذا كله وابن ذلك كله، فإن مات ورثاه ،نصفين وإن جنى عقل عواقلهما نصفين، وإن جنت الأمة فعلى صاحب العشر عشر موجب الجناية وعلى الآخر تسعة أعشار موجبها، وكذا أولادها لهما على هذا. ولو أن رجلين اشتريا عبداً ليس له نسب معروف أحدهما عشرة والآخر تسعة أعشاره ثم ادعياه معاً فهو ابنهما لا يفضل أحدهما على صاحبه في النسب، فإن جنى فجنايته على عواقلهما أعشاراً كذا في الظهيرية.
And he (the author) made it absolute that she is jointly owned between them and did not restrict it to equality in share because if she were between two, one having one-tenth and the other nine-tenths, and she bears a child and they both claim him, then he is their son—this one's whole son and that one's whole son. If he dies, they inherit him equally; and if he commits a tort, his 'aqila (tribal guarantors) bear half each; and if the slavewoman commits a tort, then the owner of one-tenth bears one-tenth of the penalty and the other bears nine-tenths of her penalty, and likewise her children for them on this basis. And if two men buy a slave without known lineage, one paying one-tenth and the other nine-tenths, and then they both claim him, he is their son—no one is preferred over the other in lineage. If he commits a tort, his tort is on their 'aqila proportionally. Thus in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage clarifies that even when co-owners have unequal shares in a slavewoman, if both claim the child, the child is equally the son of both in terms of lineage (not proportionate). Inheritance between them is equal, and in case of tort, the diyah is shared equally. However, for the mother's tort, liability is proportional to shares. The same applies if two men buy a slave without known lineage and both claim him: he is their son equally, but for his own tort, the 'aqila bear proportionally (by shares). This shows that lineage is established equally regardless of ownership share, but financial liability follows ownership.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqila): The group of male agnatic relatives who collectively pay blood money on behalf of a member. In this lesson, the 'aqila for the child includes the two co-owners as the 'asaba (residuary heirs).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two co-owners have unequal shares in a slavewoman (one 1/10, other 9/10) and both claim the child. How is inheritance distributed between them if the child dies?
A) In proportion to their shares in the mother.
B) Equally (half each).
C) The one with larger share inherits more.
D) The child inherits nothing from either.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, joint paternity establishes equal lineage regardless of ownership shares, but financial liability depends on whether it arises from the child's own act or from the mother's act as property.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Number of Claimants for Paternity
Intra-Hanafi opinions on multiple paternity claimants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar on the maximum number of claimants for paternity, and identify the practical difference in their rulings.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows any number of claimants; his students differ, creating a spectrum from two to five.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf concerning the maximum number of individuals who can simultaneously claim paternity (nasab) of one child born to a joint slavewoman. The dispute reveals how different Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar—interpreted the underlying legal principles, leading to a range of rules from unlimited claimants to a strict limit of two. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how a single legal issue can generate multiple valid opinions within a madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the passage addresses a specific scenario: multiple men (and possibly women) claim paternity of a child born to a joint slavewoman. The question is: from how many claimants can the child's nasab be established?
The baseline ruling in the matn restricts claimants to two, which opens the door for ikhtilaf on more than two.
2
List each imam's position
Write down the exact number allowed by each: Abu Hanifa: any number. Abu Yusuf: two or five (but not three). Muhammad: exactly three. Zufar: only two (also the narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad). Note also the positions on women claimants.
Pay attention to the exceptions: Abu Yusuf's 'five but not three' is unusual.
3
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For each position, determine how the ruling affects inheritance, marriage, and custody rights. For example, under Abu Hanifa, more people inherit from the child; under Zufar, only two.
The dispute also extends to whether women can establish paternity—Abu Hanifa allows it, the Sahibayn do not.
4
Evaluate the epistemological weight
Note that the text presents these as valid opinions within the Hanafi school. The mufta bihi (operative position) is not explicitly stated here, but later sources may prefer Abu Yusuf or Muhammad's view for practical reasons.
This is a textbook example of ikhtilaf within the madhhab, not a clash with outside schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بكونهما اثنين للاختلاف فيما زاد عليهما، فعند أبي حنيفة يثبت النسب من المدعيين وإن كثروا . وقال أبو يوسف : يثبت نسبه من اثنين خمسة ولا يثبت نسبه من الثلاثة . وعند محمد يثبت من الثلاثة لا غير. وقال زفر : يثبت من فقط وهو رواية الحسن بن زياد عن الإمام. وفي غاية البيان: لو تنازع فيه امرأتان قضى به أيضاً بينهما عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما لا يقضي للمرأتين، وكذلك يثبت عند أبي حنيفة للخمس. ولو تنازع فيه رجل وامرأتان يقضي به بينهم عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد يقضي للرجل ولا يقضي للمرأتين. وإذا تنازع فيه رجلان وامرأتان كل رجل يدعي هذه المرأة والمرأة لا تصدقه على ذلك فعند أبي حنيفة يقضي بين الرجلين ولا يقضي بين المرأتين .اهـ.
And he restricted it to two because of the difference of opinion on more than two. According to Abu Hanifa, lineage is established from the claimants even if they are many. Abu Yusuf said: His lineage is established from two or five, but not from three. According to Muhammad, it is established from three only. Zufar said: It is established from two only, and this is the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad from the Imam. In Ghayat al-Bayan: If two women dispute over him, it is also decided between them according to Abu Hanifa, but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not decided between the women. Similarly, it is established for five according to Abu Hanifa. If a man and two women dispute, he decides between them according to Abu Hanifa, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he decides for the man and not for the women. If two men and two women dispute, each man claiming this woman and the woman not confirming him, then according to Abu Hanifa he decides between the two men and not between the two women.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage records a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the maximum number of people who can simultaneously claim paternity of one child from a joint slavewoman. Abu Hanifa holds that the child can be attributed to any number of claimants (even more than two). Abu Yusuf allows two or five, but not three. Muhammad allows exactly three. Zufar restricts it to two. The dispute extends to cases where women are among the claimants: Abu Hanifa allows women to establish paternity (i.e., the child is attributed to them), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not allow women to establish paternity against a male claimant. This shows the range of opinions within the Hanafi school and the principle that lineage is typically attributed to males, but Abu Hanifa allows it for women if there is a claim.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity, established by claim or evidence.
يثبت (yathbutu): It is established (valid).
المدعين (al-mudda'een): The claimants (those who claim paternity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, from how many claimants can paternity be established?
A) Only two.
B) Only three.
C) Two or five.
D) Any number.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains a spectrum of opinions on the maximum number of paternity claimants, ranging from unlimited (Abu Hanifa) to strictly two (Zufar), with intermediate positions by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Service, Freedom, and Manumission of Jointly Owned Umm Walad
Rulings on service, freedom, and compensation for joint umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on service, freedom upon death, and manumission of a jointly owned umm walad, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on sa'ya (compensation work).

Core Idea
A jointly owned umm walad serves each owner and becomes free upon any owner's death or manumission, but compensation duty differs between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

When two men own an umm walad jointly, her status as mother of each owner's child creates unique rights. She serves each alternately. But what happens when one owner dies or manumits her? Does she become fully free? Must she work for compensation? The Hanafi masters disagree on the details, and this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the joint umm walad's service scheme
The passage states she serves each owner one day. Record this as the default arrangement.
No dispute on service rotation.
2
Apply the ruling upon death of one owner
When one owner dies, she becomes free. There is no liability on the living owner towards the dead owner's estate because both consented to her freedom after death.
This is unanimous among the Hanafis.
3
Identify the ikhtilaf on sa'ya after death
Abu Hanifa holds she does not perform sa'ya (work to pay) for the living owner because she has no value according to him. The Sahibayn require sa'ya for half her value.
Key difference: valuation of umm walad.
4
Apply the ruling upon manumission by one owner
If one owner manumits her, she becomes free entirely. Abu Hanifa says no liability on the manumitter and no sa'ya. The Sahibayn say the manumitter is liable if wealthy, and she performs sa'ya if he is poor.
Same pattern of ikhtilaf.
5
Relate to the divisibility of emancipation
The passage concludes that emancipation of a regular female slave (qin) can be divisible (only the manumitter's share frees, with sa'ya for the rest), but emancipation of an umm walad is not divisible because it automatically frees the whole.
This is agreed upon by all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد بكونها أم ولد لهما أنها تخدم كلاً منهما يوماً وإذا مات أحدهما عتقت ولا ضمان للحي في تركة الميت لرضا كل منهما بعتقها بعد الموت، ولا تسعى للحي أبي حنيفة لعدم تقومها، وعلى قولهما تسعى في نصف قيمتها له. ولو أعتقها أحدهما عتقت ولا ضمان عليه للساكت ولا سعاية في قول أبي حنيفة وعلى قولهما يضمن إن كان موسراً وتسعى إن كان معسراً؛ كذا في فتح القدير. فعلى هذا محل قول الإمام العتق يتجزأ في القنة، أما في أم الولد فعتقها لا يتجزأ اتفاقاً وقد نبه عليه في المجتبى.
And it is useful that she being umm walad for both means she serves each one day. If one dies, she becomes free, and there is no liability on the living from the dead's estate because each consents to her freedom after death. According to Abu Hanifa, she does not perform sa'ya (work to pay off) for the living because she has no value according to him; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she performs sa'ya for half her value to him. If one manumits her, she becomes free, and there is no liability on him towards the silent partner, and no sa'ya according to Abu Hanifa; according to them, he is liable if wealthy, and she performs sa'ya if he is poor. So in Fath al-Qadir. Based on this, the Imam's statement that emancipation can be divisible (yatajazzah) applies to a female slave (qin), but in the case of umm walad, her emancipation is not divisible by agreement, as pointed out in al-Mujtaba.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage details the practical consequences of joint umm walad status. She serves each owner alternate days. Upon the death of one owner, her share becomes free, and the living owner does not owe compensation because both had consented to her freedom upon death (via the ta'dbir implicit in umm walad status). According to Abu Hanifa, the surviving owner does not require her to work for compensation because she cannot be valued. According to the Sahibayn, she must work for half her value. Similarly, if one owner manumits her, she becomes fully free; if the manumitter is wealthy, he must pay the other's share; if poor, she works for it. This leads to the principle: emancipation of a regular female slave is divisible (one can free his share), but emancipation of an umm walad is not divisible because it would automatically free the whole, as agreed upon by all.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
سعاية (sa'ya): Working to earn money to pay compensation for freedom.
تجزأ (yatajazzah): Divisible; emancipation can be partial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if one co-owner manumits the jointly owned umm walad, what is the situation?
A) She is free and the manumitter owes the other's share.
B) She is free and the silent partner gets nothing.
C) She is free and must work for the silent partner.
D) The manumission is invalid without the other's consent.

Key Takeaway
A jointly owned umm walad becomes fully free upon the death or manumission of any owner, but whether she must work for compensation depends on the Hanafi imam followed.

Divisibility of Emancipation in Umm Walad, Mudabbir, and Mukatab
Refining the rule: umm walad exception and mudabbir/mukatab divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the divisibility of emancipation for umm walad, mudabbir, and mukatab and explain the Hanafi reasoning using qiyas.

Core Idea
Emancipation is divisible for ordinary slaves, but for umm walad it is indivisible; mudabbir and mukatab are divisible despite their special status.

The Hanafi school holds that emancipation of a joint slave can be partial for a regular slave (qinn). However, this rule changes for an umm walad, whose emancipation is indivisible by consensus. The present passage refines that rule: the mudabbir and mukatab are not like the umm walad; their emancipation is divisible. The author uses qiyas from earlier rulings to prove this, citing the non-transfer of wala' for mudabbir and the partner's options for mukatab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three slave types and their emancipation rulings
List the three categories: qinn (regular slave), umm walad, mudabbir, and mukatab. Note the default rule of divisibility and the exception for umm walad.
The passage focuses on the exception and the clarification that mudabbir and mukatab revert to the default.
2
Trace the qiyas for mudabbir
Examine the mudabbir case: when one joint owner frees his share, the mudabbir becomes free but the wala' remains with the mudabbir (not the manumitter). This proves the manumission is only partial, hence divisible.
The wala' non-change is the key indicator.
3
Trace the qiyas for mukatab
In the mukatab case, the partner has a choice between requiring the manumitter to pay compensation (ta'dhim) or demanding the mukatab to earn (sa'ya) for half the value. This freedom of choice demonstrates the emancipation is partial.
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on the exact guarantee amount between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
4
Contrast with umm walad
Unlike mudabbir and mukatab, the umm walad's emancipation is indivisible by agreement. If any owner frees his share, she becomes fully free and the other owner is entitled to compensation.
This rule is a form of takhsis (exception) to the general qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى هذا محل قول الإمام العتق يتجزأ في القنة، أما في أم الولد فعتقها لا يتجزأ اتفاقاً وقد نبه عليه في المجتبى. وفي البدائع : وإن كانت الأنصباء مختلفة بأن كان لأحدهم السدس وللآخر الربع وللآخر الثلث وللآخر ما بقي أنه ابنه من عند فعتقها لا يتجزئ اتفاقاً) لم يتعرض لإعتاق المدبر والمكاتب وتخصيصه بأم الولد يفيد تجزي إعتاق المدبر والمكاتب، أما المدبر فيدل عليه ما قدمه في بابه عند قوله فلا يباع ولا يوهب من أنه لو كان المدبر بين اثنين أعتقه أحدهما وهو موسر وضمن قيمة نصيب شركه عتق المدبر ولم يتغير الولاء لأن العتق ها هنا ثبت من جهة المدبر في الحقيقة لا من جهة المعتق لأن المعتق بأداء الضمان لا يملك نصيب الشريك ها هنا لأن المدبر لا يقبل الانتقال الخ فعدم تغير الولاء أي بقاؤه بين المدبر والمعتق دليل على أنه لم يعتق كله من جهة المعتق وإلا كان الولاء له. أما المكاتب فيدل عليه ما في كافي الحاكم من أنه إذا كاتبا عبدهما ثم أعتقه أحدهما جاز والمكاتب بالخيار إن شاء عجز ويكون الشريك بالخيار بين التضمين وبين السعاية في نصف القيمة والعتق عنده وقال أبو يوسف يضمن نصف قيمته لو موسراً. وقال محمد: يضمن الأقل من نصف القيمة ونصف ما بقي من المكاتبة. وإن لم يعجز حتى عن مال كثير أخذ الذي لم يعتق نصف المكاتبة من ماله والباقي لورثته. فهذا صريح في أن إعتاق المكاتب يتجزى عنده ولذا تخير الشريك بين الاستسعاء والعتق والله أعلم.
Based on this, the Imam's statement that emancipation can be divisible applies to a female slave (qinn), but in the case of umm walad, her emancipation is not divisible by agreement, as pointed out in al-Mujtaba. In al-Bada'i: Even if the shares are different—one having one-sixth, another one-fourth, another one-third, and the other the remainder—that he is his son? So her emancipation is not divisible by agreement. He did not address the emancipation of the mudabbir and mukatab, and his specification of umm walad indicates that the emancipation of mudabbir and mukatab is divisible. As for the mudabbir, it is indicated by what he presented in its chapter at his statement 'so he may not be sold or given as a gift' that if the mudabbir is jointly owned by two, and one manumits him while wealthy and guarantees the value of the partner's share, then the mudabbir becomes free and the wala' does not change, because the emancipation here is actually from the mudabbir, not from the manumitter, because the manumitter by paying the guarantee does not own the partner's share here because the mudabbir is not transferable. So the non-change of wala'—i.e., it remains between the mudabbir and the manumitter—proves that he was not fully freed by the manumitter, otherwise the wala' would belong to him. As for the mukatab, it is indicated by what is in Kafi al-Hakim: if both (owners) kitaba their slave, then one manumits him, it is valid, and the mukatab has the option to nullify the kitaba, and the partner has the choice between guaranteeing or sa'ya for half the value, and the emancipation is according to him (Abu Hanifa?). Abu Yusuf said: He guarantees half his value if wealthy. Muhammad said: He guarantees the lesser of half the value and half of what remains of the kitaba. If he does not nullify until after much wealth, the one who did not manumit takes half the kitaba from his wealth, and the rest goes to his heirs. This is explicit that the emancipation of a mukatab is divisible according to him (Abu Hanifa?), and therefore the partner chooses between sa'ya and emancipation. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage refines the principle of divisibility of emancipation (tajazzu' al-'itq). It establishes that for a regular slave (qinn), emancipation can be partial: one owner can free his share, and the slave remains partly enslaved until the other shares are freed. However, for an umm walad, emancipation is indivisible: if one owner frees his share, the entire slavewoman becomes free, and the other owner must be compensated. The text explains that this does not apply to a mudabbir (a slave promised freedom upon master's death) or a mukatab (a slave under contract of kitaba). For a mudabbir, if one owner manumits, the mudabbir becomes free, but the wala' (clientage) remains divided between the mudabbir and the manumitter, proving partial emancipation. For a mukatab, the same logic applies: the manumission is partial, and the partner has options. This shows the Hanafi commitment to analogical reasoning (qiyas) in deriving rules from precedents.

Key Terms
تجزئ العتق (tajazzu' al-'itq): The divisibility of emancipation: whether one can free only his share of a jointly owned slave.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby gaining certain rights and protections.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a kitaba contract to earn his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true regarding the divisibility of emancipation?
A) Umm walad is divisible; mudabbir is indivisible.
B) Mudabbir and mukatab are divisible; umm walad is indivisible.
C) All three types are divisible.
D) Only the regular slave (qinn) is divisible; the three types are indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school makes a distinction: the emancipation of an umm walad is indivisible, but for a mudabbir or mukatab it is divisible, proven by qiyas from the non-transfer of wala' and partner's options.

Master's Claim to Child of Mukatab's Slave-Girl: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on paternity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a master claiming paternity of a child from his mukatab's slave-girl and state the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the need for the mukatab's confirmation.

Core Idea
A master's claim to paternity of his mukatab's slave-girl's child requires the mukatab's confirmation; without it, lineage is not established.

In Hanafi fiqh, the relationship between a master and his mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) has special rules. One such rule concerns paternity claims: if a master claims the child born to his mukatab's slave-girl, does the mukatab's confirmation matter? This lesson unpacks the ruling and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, focusing on the legal reasoning behind the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the parties: master, mukatab, and the mukatab's slave-girl. The master claims the child born to her.
The mukatab owns his earnings (kasb) but the slave-girl's person is owned by the mukatab, not the master.
2
State the ruling with confirmation
If the mukatab confirms the claim, lineage is established. The master must pay 'aqr (compensation) and the child's value at birth. The slave-girl does not become umm walad.
'Aqr is compensation for intercourse without full ownership.
3
State the ruling without confirmation and Abu Yusuf's view
If the mukatab denies, per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad lineage is not established. Abu Yusuf argues it is established without confirmation, analogizing to a father claiming his son's slave-girl's child.
Abu Yusuf's qiyas is the key point of dispute.
4
Identify the distinction (farq)
The majority distinguish because the master does not own the mukatab's kasb, while a father can own his son's kasb. Thus the father's claim is stronger.
This distinction (farq) is known as 'adam milk al-kasb'.
5
Determine the consequences
Consequences include: no umm walad status for the slave-girl, liability for 'aqr and value, and if the master later owns her, lineage is established (since the bar is removed).
The child is freed and the master owes its value assessed at birth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو ادعى ولد أمة مكاتبه وصدقة المكاتب لزم النسب والعقر وقيمة الولد ولم تصر أم ولده وإن كذبه لم يثبت) عند أبي يوسف أنه يثبت النسب بدون تصديقه اعتباراً بالأب يدعي ولد جارية ابنه وجه الظاهر وهو الفرق أن المولى لا يملك التصرف في إكساب لا يتملكه والأب يملك تملكه فلا يعتبر تصديق الابن وإنما لزمه العقر لأنه لا يتقدمه الملك لأن ماله من الحق كافٍ لصحة الاستيلاد لما ذكر، وإنما لزمه قيمة الولد لأنه في معنى المغرور حيث اعتمد دليلاً وهو أنه كسب كسبه فلم يرض برقه فيكون حراباً لقيمة ثابت النسب منه إلا أن القيمة هنا تعتبر يوم ولد وقيمة ولد المغرور يوم الخصومة. وإنما لم تصر الجارية أم ولد للمولى لأنه لا ملك له فيها حقيقة كما في ولد المغرور، وإن كذبه المكاتب في النسب لم يثبت من المولى لما بينا أنه لا بد من تصديقه فلو ملكه يوماً ثبت نسبه منه لقيام الموجب وزوال حق المكاتب إذ هو المانع قيد بأمة المكاتب لأنه لو وطئ المكاتبة فجاءت بولد فادعاه ثبت نسبه، ولا يشترط تصديقها لأن رقبتها مملوكة له بخلاف كسبها. وفي التبيين : ولو ولدت منه جارية غيره وقال أحلها لي مولاها والولد ولدي فصدقه المولى في الإحلال وكذبه في الولد لم يثبت نسبه، فإن ملكها يوماً ثبت نسبه وصارت أم ولد له، ولو صدقه في الولد ثبت نسبه، ولو استولد جارية أحد أبويه أو امرأته وقال ظننت أنها تحل لي لم يثبت نسبه منه ولا حد عليه وإن ملكه يوماً عتق عليه وإن ملك أمه لا تصير أم ولد له لعدم ثبوت نسبه اهـ . والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.

قوله: (وهي ليست كأم ولد لواحد الخ) أقول الظاهر أن الضمير راجع لأصل المسألة. إذا ادعياه معاً ولا مرجح حتى ثبت نسبه منهما لأنها تبقى مشتركة بينهما فلا يحل وطؤها لأحدهما بخلاف ما إذا وجد المرجح بأن حملت على ملك أحدهما نكاحاً أو رقبة حتى ثبت من الأرجح وهو الزوج والمالك الأول وتصير أم ولد له فلم تبق مشتركة. ويدل لما قلنا أنه في المجتبى قال في تعليل أصل المسألة : ولأنهما استويا في سبب الاستحقاق فيستويان فيه حتى لو وجد المرجح لا يثبت منهما بأن كان أحدهما أب الآخر أو كان مسلماً والآخر ذمياً ثبت من الأب والمسلم لوجود المرجح، ولما ثبت نسبه منهما صارت أم ولد لهما ويقع عقرهما قصاصاً، ولو جاءت بآخر لم يثبت نسبه من واحد إلا بالدعوى لأن الوطء حرام فتعتبر الدعوة ا هـ. فقوله ولما ثبت نسبه منهما الخ صريح في رجوعه لأصل المسألة فتنبه لذلك فإنه مما خفي على كثيرين ولم أر من نبه عليه والله سبحانه أعلم.
He said: (If he claims the child of his mukatab's female slave and the mukatab confirms [the claim], then lineage, 'aqr (compensation for intercourse), and the value of the child become incumbent, and she does not become umm walad for him. If the mukatab denies [the claim], it is not established.) According to Abu Yusuf, lineage is established without his confirmation, by analogy with a father claiming the child of his son's slave-girl. The apparent view and the distinction is that the master does not have authority to dispose of the earnings (kasb) that he does not own, whereas the father can own it, so the son's confirmation is not considered. He is liable for 'aqr because his ownership does not precede [the intercourse], for his right to the kasb is sufficient for the validity of istilad as mentioned. He is liable for the value of the child because he is like the deceived (maghrur), having relied on the evidence that [the slave] is his kasb, so he was not content with her enslavement, so the child becomes free and he owes its value, except that here the value is assessed on the day of birth, while the value for the maghrur is assessed on the day of litigation. She does not become umm walad for the master because he has no actual ownership over her, as in the case of the child of the maghrur. If the mukatab denies the lineage, it is not established from the master because, as we explained, his confirmation is necessary. But if he acquires ownership of her for a day, lineage is established from him because the cause (mujib) is present and the right of the mukatab (which was a bar) is removed. The restriction to the mukatab's slave-girl is because if he had intercourse with a mukatabah and she bore a child and he claimed it, lineage is established, and her confirmation is not required because her person (raqabah) is owned by him, unlike her earnings. In al-Tabyin: If his slave-girl gave birth to a child from another man, and the master said, 'Her master made her lawful for me, and the child is mine,' and the master (of the other) confirmed the making lawful but denied the child, his lineage is not established. But if he owns her for a day, his lineage is established and she becomes umm walad for him. If he confirmed the child, lineage is established. If he claimed istilad of the slave-girl of one of his parents or his wife, saying 'I thought she was lawful for me,' lineage is not established, and there is no hadd on him, but if he owns her for a day, she becomes free, and if he owns the mother, she does not become umm walad because of the non-establishment of lineage. End. And Allah Most High knows best.

He said: (And she is not like the umm walad of one owner...) I say: Apparently the pronoun refers back to the original issue. If they both claim [paternity] together with no preponderating factor, lineage is established from both of them because she remains shared between them, so intercourse with her is not permissible for either. This contrasts with the case where a preponderating factor exists, such as if she conceived while in the ownership of one of them through marriage or slavery, so lineage is established from the preponderant, who is the husband and the first owner, and she becomes his umm walad, so she is no longer shared. Evidence for what we say is in al-Mujtaba, where in explaining the original issue it is said: Because they are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they are equal in it. So if a preponderating factor exists, it is not established from both; e.g., if one is the father of the other, or one is Muslim and the other dhimmi, it is established from the father and the Muslim due to the existence of the preponderating factor. And since lineage is established from both, she becomes umm walad for both, and their 'aqr falls reciprocally (qisas). If she subsequently bears another child, its lineage is not established from either without a claim, because intercourse is unlawful, so the claim is considered. End. So his words 'and since lineage is established from both' etc. clearly refer back to the original issue, so pay attention, for this has been hidden from many, and I have not seen anyone who pointed it out. And Allah Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a master who claims paternity of a child born to his mukatab's slave-girl. The text states the ruling: if the mukatab confirms the claim, the master incurs the 'aqr (compensation), the child's value at birth, and the child's lineage is established, but the slave-girl does not become umm walad. If the mukatab denies, lineage is not established. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that lineage is established without the mukatab's confirmation, analogizing to a father claiming the child of his son's slave-girl. The commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym or similar) explains the distinction: the master does not own the mukatab's earnings (kasb), while the son owns his earnings, so the father's claim is accepted over the son's. The 'aqr is due because the master did not own the slave-girl at the time of intercourse; the value of the child is due because the master is like a deceived party (maghrur) who relied on the evidence that the slave was his kasb. The child is freed and the master owes its value (assessed at birth, unlike the maghrur case where it is assessed at litigation). The master does not have actual ownership over the slave-girl, so she does not become umm walad. If the master later acquires ownership of her for even a day, lineage is established because the bar (mukatab's right) is removed. The commentator then addresses a related point from the matn: 'She is not like the umm walad of one owner when two claim paternity together.' If both master and mukatab claim paternity with no preponderating factor, lineage is established from both, and she becomes a shared umm walad, making intercourse impermissible for either. If a preponderating factor exists (e.g., one is father, or one is Muslim while the other is dhimmi), lineage is established only from the preponderant. This highlights the Hanafi rules of joint claims and preponderance (tarjih).

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, paying installments to earn freedom.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and the master has claimed the child's paternity. She becomes free upon the master's death.
العقر (al-'aqr): Compensation paid for intercourse with a female slave one does not fully own, typically the difference between her value as a virgin and as a non-virgin.
المغرور (al-maghrur): One who is deceived; in this context, a person who had intercourse with a slave-girl believing she was his own, but it turns out she was not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is required for a master's claim to paternity of a child from his mukatab's slave-girl to be established?
A) The master must own the slave-girl at the time of birth.
B) The mukatab must confirm the claim.
C) The child must be born more than six months after the marriage.
D) The master must have no other children.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a master's claim to paternity of his mukatab's slave-girl's child requires the mukatab's confirmation, and the slave-girl does not become umm walad.

Joint Claim to Paternity by Master and Mukatab: Preponderance Rules
Ruling on joint paternity claims with preponderance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule when both the master and the mukatab claim paternity of the same child from a slave-girl, and identify when preponderance (tarjih) determines exclusive lineage.

Core Idea
When two claimants are equal in cause, lineage is established from both; preponderance (e.g., one is father, one is Muslim) breaks the tie.

In the previous lesson, we saw the ruling when only the master claims paternity. But what if both the master and the mukatab claim the same child? This lesson addresses that scenario, including the effect of preponderating factors such as one claimant being the father of the other or one being a Muslim and the other a dhimmi. The passage clarifies that the slave-girl becomes a shared umm walad, making intercourse impermissible for both, unless a preponderating factor exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of joint claim
Both the master and the mukatab claim paternity of the same child born to the mukatab's slave-girl. No preponderating factor exists initially.
The slave-girl is shared between them in terms of reproductive rights.
2
State the baseline ruling
When both claim and no preponderating factor, lineage is established from both. The slave-girl becomes umm walad for both, and intercourse with her is prohibited for each.
She becomes a shared umm walad, which is a unique status.
3
Identify preponderating factors
Factors that break the tie: one claimant is the father of the other (father's claim prevails), or one is Muslim and the other dhimmi (Muslim's claim prevails).
These factors give one claimant a stronger right based on precedence or religious honor.
4
Determine consequences with preponderance
Only the preponderant claimant's lineage is established; the other's claim is rejected. The slave-girl becomes umm walad solely for the preponderant claimant.
The other claimant loses his umm walad rights.
5
Note subsequent births
If the slave-girl later bears another child, its lineage is not established from either without a separate claim, because intercourse is unlawful (due to the dispute).
The requirement of a separate claim emphasizes the prohibition of intercourse while ownership is contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو ادعى ولد أمة مكاتبه وصدقة المكاتب لزم النسب والعقر وقيمة الولد ولم تصر أم ولده وإن كذبه لم يثبت) عند أبي يوسف أنه يثبت النسب بدون تصديقه اعتباراً بالأب يدعي ولد جارية ابنه وجه الظاهر وهو الفرق أن المولى لا يملك التصرف في إكساب لا يتملكه والأب يملك تملكه فلا يعتبر تصديق الابن وإنما لزمه العقر لأنه لا يتقدمه الملك لأن ماله من الحق كافٍ لصحة الاستيلاد لما ذكر، وإنما لزمه قيمة الولد لأنه في معنى المغرور حيث اعتمد دليلاً وهو أنه كسب كسبه فلم يرض برقه فيكون حراباً لقيمة ثابت النسب منه إلا أن القيمة هنا تعتبر يوم ولد وقيمة ولد المغرور يوم الخصومة. وإنما لم تصر الجارية أم ولد للمولى لأنه لا ملك له فيها حقيقة كما في ولد المغرور، وإن كذبه المكاتب في النسب لم يثبت من المولى لما بينا أنه لا بد من تصديقه فلو ملكه يوماً ثبت نسبه منه لقيام الموجب وزوال حق المكاتب إذ هو المانع قيد بأمة المكاتب لأنه لو وطئ المكاتبة فجاءت بولد فادعاه ثبت نسبه، ولا يشترط تصديقها لأن رقبتها مملوكة له بخلاف كسبها. وفي التبيين : ولو ولدت منه جارية غيره وقال أحلها لي مولاها والولد ولدي فصدقه المولى في الإحلال وكذبه في الولد لم يثبت نسبه، فإن ملكها يوماً ثبت نسبه وصارت أم ولد له، ولو صدقه في الولد ثبت نسبه، ولو استولد جارية أحد أبويه أو امرأته وقال ظننت أنها تحل لي لم يثبت نسبه منه ولا حد عليه وإن ملكه يوماً عتق عليه وإن ملك أمه لا تصير أم ولد له لعدم ثبوت نسبه اهـ . والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.

قوله: (وهي ليست كأم ولد لواحد الخ) أقول الظاهر أن الضمير راجع لأصل المسألة. إذا ادعياه معاً ولا مرجح حتى ثبت نسبه منهما لأنها تبقى مشتركة بينهما فلا يحل وطؤها لأحدهما بخلاف ما إذا وجد المرجح بأن حملت على ملك أحدهما نكاحاً أو رقبة حتى ثبت من الأرجح وهو الزوج والمالك الأول وتصير أم ولد له فلم تبق مشتركة. ويدل لما قلنا أنه في المجتبى قال في تعليل أصل المسألة : ولأنهما استويا في سبب الاستحقاق فيستويان فيه حتى لو وجد المرجح لا يثبت منهما بأن كان أحدهما أب الآخر أو كان مسلماً والآخر ذمياً ثبت من الأب والمسلم لوجود المرجح، ولما ثبت نسبه منهما صارت أم ولد لهما ويقع عقرهما قصاصاً، ولو جاءت بآخر لم يثبت نسبه من واحد إلا بالدعوى لأن الوطء حرام فتعتبر الدعوة ا هـ. فقوله ولما ثبت نسبه منهما الخ صريح في رجوعه لأصل المسألة فتنبه لذلك فإنه مما خفي على كثيرين ولم أر من نبه عليه والله سبحانه أعلم.
He said: (If he claims the child of his mukatab's female slave and the mukatab confirms [the claim], then lineage, 'aqr (compensation for intercourse), and the value of the child become incumbent, and she does not become umm walad for him. If the mukatab denies [the claim], it is not established.) According to Abu Yusuf, lineage is established without his confirmation, by analogy with a father claiming the child of his son's slave-girl. The apparent view and the distinction is that the master does not have authority to dispose of the earnings (kasb) that he does not own, whereas the father can own it, so the son's confirmation is not considered. He is liable for 'aqr because his ownership does not precede [the intercourse], for his right to the kasb is sufficient for the validity of istilad as mentioned. He is liable for the value of the child because he is like the deceived (maghrur), having relied on the evidence that [the slave] is his kasb, so he was not content with her enslavement, so the child becomes free and he owes its value, except that here the value is assessed on the day of birth, while the value for the maghrur is assessed on the day of litigation. She does not become umm walad for the master because he has no actual ownership over her, as in the case of the child of the maghrur. If the mukatab denies the lineage, it is not established from the master because, as we explained, his confirmation is necessary. But if he acquires ownership of her for a day, lineage is established from him because the cause (mujib) is present and the right of the mukatab (which was a bar) is removed. The restriction to the mukatab's slave-girl is because if he had intercourse with a mukatabah and she bore a child and he claimed it, lineage is established, and her confirmation is not required because her person (raqabah) is owned by him, unlike her earnings. In al-Tabyin: If his slave-girl gave birth to a child from another man, and the master said, 'Her master made her lawful for me, and the child is mine,' and the master (of the other) confirmed the making lawful but denied the child, his lineage is not established. But if he owns her for a day, his lineage is established and she becomes umm walad for him. If he confirmed the child, lineage is established. If he claimed istilad of the slave-girl of one of his parents or his wife, saying 'I thought she was lawful for me,' lineage is not established, and there is no hadd on him, but if he owns her for a day, she becomes free, and if he owns the mother, she does not become umm walad because of the non-establishment of lineage. End. And Allah Most High knows best.

He said: (And she is not like the umm walad of one owner...) I say: Apparently the pronoun refers back to the original issue. If they both claim [paternity] together with no preponderating factor, lineage is established from both of them because she remains shared between them, so intercourse with her is not permissible for either. This contrasts with the case where a preponderating factor exists, such as if she conceived while in the ownership of one of them through marriage or slavery, so lineage is established from the preponderant, who is the husband and the first owner, and she becomes his umm walad, so she is no longer shared. Evidence for what we say is in al-Mujtaba, where in explaining the original issue it is said: Because they are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they are equal in it. So if a preponderating factor exists, it is not established from both; e.g., if one is the father of the other, or one is Muslim and the other dhimmi, it is established from the father and the Muslim due to the existence of the preponderating factor. And since lineage is established from both, she becomes umm walad for both, and their 'aqr falls reciprocally (qisas). If she subsequently bears another child, its lineage is not established from either without a claim, because intercourse is unlawful, so the claim is considered. End. So his words 'and since lineage is established from both' etc. clearly refer back to the original issue, so pay attention, for this has been hidden from many, and I have not seen anyone who pointed it out. And Allah Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a master who claims paternity of a child born to his mukatab's slave-girl. The text states the ruling: if the mukatab confirms the claim, the master incurs the 'aqr (compensation), the child's value at birth, and the child's lineage is established, but the slave-girl does not become umm walad. If the mukatab denies, lineage is not established. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that lineage is established without the mukatab's confirmation, analogizing to a father claiming the child of his son's slave-girl. The commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym or similar) explains the distinction: the master does not own the mukatab's earnings (kasb), while the son owns his earnings, so the father's claim is accepted over the son's. The 'aqr is due because the master did not own the slave-girl at the time of intercourse; the value of the child is due because the master is like a deceived party (maghrur) who relied on the evidence that the slave was his kasb. The child is freed and the master owes its value (assessed at birth, unlike the maghrur case where it is assessed at litigation). The master does not have actual ownership over the slave-girl, so she does not become umm walad. If the master later acquires ownership of her for even a day, lineage is established because the bar (mukatab's right) is removed. The commentator then addresses a related point from the matn: 'She is not like the umm walad of one owner when two claim paternity together.' If both master and mukatab claim paternity with no preponderating factor, lineage is established from both, and she becomes a shared umm walad, making intercourse impermissible for either. If a preponderating factor exists (e.g., one is father, or one is Muslim while the other is dhimmi), lineage is established only from the preponderant. This highlights the Hanafi rules of joint claims and preponderance (tarjih).

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih): A preponderating factor that gives one claimant priority over another, e.g., being a father or Muslim.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Entitlement or right; in this context, the right to claim paternity based on ownership or status.
عقر قصاصاً ('aqr qisasan): The 'aqr compensation offsets between the two claimants (like qisas, reciprocal). When both are liable, the debts cancel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If both the master and the mukatab claim paternity of the same child with no preponderating factor, what is the outcome?
A) Lineage is established from the master only, as he has superior authority.
B) Lineage is established from both, and the slave-girl becomes umm walad for both.
C) Lineage is not established from either until a judge decides.
D) The child is considered free and belongs to the state.

Key Takeaway
When two claimants are equal in cause, lineage is established from both, and the slave-girl becomes a shared umm walad; preponderance (e.g., fatherhood or Islam) breaks the equality.

Defining Tasarri and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Definition of tasarri and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of tasarri according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf, including its practical effect on oaths.

Core Idea
Tasarri is intercourse with a concubine regardless of withdrawal; Abu Yusuf restricts it to intercourse with emission.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines the legal term 'tasarri' and resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute. Understanding this definition is crucial for rulings on conditional oaths involving concubinage. The commentator also warns against a common error: analogizing the suspension of divorce to the suspension of manumission upon tasarri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage begins with a quote from al-Tabyin about a conditional oath: 'If I take you as a concubine, then my slave is free.' This sets the legal context for the definition of tasarri.
The condition triggers manumission upon the act of tasarri.
2
Analyze the definitional components
The passage then provides the linguistic derivation of tasarri from sirriyyah, and gives the technical meaning according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: making a slave-girl a concubine by having intercourse with her, with or without withdrawal.
Key element: intercourse is the defining act, not mere purchase or intention.
3
Compare with Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf requires that the intercourse be without withdrawal (i.e., with emission). The passage contrasts the two views.
This is the core of the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If a man swears not to take a concubine and then has intercourse with withdrawal, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that he has broken his oath; Abu Yusuf rules he has not.
The practical consequence differs based on the definition.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The passage cites al-Quduri and Fath al-Qadir in support of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position, indicating it is the relied-upon view.
Tarjih is given to the majority of the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في التبيين: لو قال لأمة إن تسريت بك فعبدي حر فاشتراها فتسرى بها عتق عبده الذي كان في ملكه وقت الحلف ولا يعتق من اشتراه بعده اهـ. فاحفظ هذا فإن بعض أهل العصر فاس مسألة تعليق الطلاق بالتسري على مسئلة المختصر وهو غلط فاحش لأن المنكوحة يصح تعليق طلاقها بأي شرط كان. ثم اعلم أن التسري هنا تفعل من السرية وهو اتخاذها والسرية إن كانت من السرور فإنها تسر بهذه الحالة ويسر هو بها، أو من السرو والسيادة فضم سينها على الأصل. وإن كانت من السر بمعنى الجماع أو بمعنى ضد الجهر فإنها قد تخفى على الزوجات الحرائر فضمها من تغييرات النسب كما قالوا دهري بالضم في النسبة إلى الدهر. وفي النسبة إلى السهل من الأرض سهلي بالضم والفعل منه بحسب اعتبار مصدره ومعنى التسري عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد أن يحصن أمته ويعدها للجماع أفضى إليها بمائه أو عزل عنها. وعند أبي يوسف أن لا يعزل ماءه ذلك فعرف أنه لو وطئ أمة له ولم يفعل ما ذكرناه من التحصين والإعداد لا يكون تسرياً وإن لم يعزل عنها وإن علقت منه، ولو حلف لا يتسرى فاشترى جارية فحصنها ووطئها حنث ؛ ذكره القدوري في التجريد عن أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
He said in al-Tabyin: If a man said to a slave-girl, 'If I take you as a concubine, then my slave is free,' then he bought her and took her as a concubine, his slave that he owned at the time of the oath is freed, and the one he bought after that is not freed. End quote. So preserve this, for some contemporary people have likened the issue of suspending divorce with tasarri to the issue of al-Mukhtasar, which is a gross error, because the suspension of divorce of a wife is valid with any condition. Then know that 'tasarri' here is a verbal noun from 'sirriyyah' which is taking her as a concubine. And 'sirriyyah' if derived from 'suroor' (joy), then she and he are joyful. Or from 'sarw' and 'siyadah', so its sin is pronounced with damm based on the original. And if derived from 'sirr' meaning intercourse or meaning secret (opposite of public), then she may be hidden from the free wives, so its damm is from the changes of lineage, as they said 'duhriyy' with damm in attribution to dahr, and in attribution to 'sahl' (plain land) 'suhliyy' with damm. And the verb according to its meaning: the meaning of tasarri according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that he makes his slave-girl a concubine and prepares her for intercourse, either by having intercourse with emission or by withdrawal. According to Abu Yusuf, it is that he does not withdraw his water (i.e., has intercourse with emission). So it is known that if he had intercourse with his slave-girl and did not do what we mentioned of making her a concubine and preparing, it is not tasarri, even if he did not withdraw and she became pregnant. If he swore not to take a concubine, then bought a slave-girl and made her a concubine and had intercourse, he breaks his oath. This is mentioned by al-Quduri in al-Tajrid from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; likewise in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage is from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discussing the meaning of 'tasarri' (taking a concubine) in the context of conditional oaths. The commentator first quotes al-Tabyin: if a man says to his slave-girl, 'If I take you as a concubine, then my slave is free,' and then he buys her and takes her as a concubine, the slave he owned at the time of the oath is freed. Slaves purchased later are not affected. This ruling is based on the fulfillment of the condition without impediment. The commentator warns against a common mistake: some contemporary scholars analogize the suspension of divorce upon the act of tasarri to the general rule in al-Mukhtasar, but this is a grave error. The suspension of divorce on a wife is valid with any condition, so such an analogy is unsound. He then delves into the etymology of 'tasarri,' explaining its possible derivations. Finally, he presents the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad define tasarri as making the slave-girl a concubine by having intercourse with her, with or without withdrawal. Abu Yusuf adds the condition that the husband must not withdraw (i.e., must have emission). Consequently, if a man swears not to take a concubine and then has intercourse without fulfilling Abu Yusuf's condition, according to Abu Yusuf he has not broken his oath, while according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad he has. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as affirmed by al-Quduri and Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
تسري (tasarri): Verbal noun meaning taking a female slave as a concubine (making her a sirriyyah). The text defines it specifically as having intercourse with a slave-girl with the intention of making her a concubine.
سرية (sirriyyah): A female slave taken as a concubine; the text discusses its etymology, possibly from 'suroor' (joy) or 'sirr' (secret).
أمة (amah): A female slave; in this context, one whom the owner may take as a concubine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what constitutes 'tasarri' (taking a concubine)?
A) Simply purchasing a slave-girl.
B) Having intercourse with a slave-girl, whether with emission or withdrawal.
C) Having intercourse with a slave-girl without withdrawal only.
D) Writing a marriage contract with a slave-girl.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of tasarri (taking a concubine) requires intercourse with the slave-girl; the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) does not require emission, unlike the view of Abu Yusuf.

The Distinction Between Oaths of Tazwij and Tazawwuj
Explains the distinction between swearing about giving in marriage and marrying oneself, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between oaths concerning tazwīj and tazawwuj and determine whether an oath is violated in a given scenario.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, commanding another to give in marriage does not violate one's oath, but commanding another to marry oneself does.

Oaths concerning marriage are frequent in legal practice. The Hanafi school makes a crucial distinction between swearing not to perform a marriage for another (tazwīj) and swearing not to marry oneself (tazawwuj). This lesson unpacks that distinction through the explanations of Najm al-Din and the rulings in al-Bada'i.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of oaths
Identify the key terms: tazwīj (giving someone in marriage) and tazawwuj (marrying oneself). Note that the oath formula uses these words.
The difference hinges on whose action is attributed.
2
Learn the general rule from Najm al-Din
Najm al-Din states: 'Tazwīj by command does not attach its ruling to him; tazawwuj by command does attach its ruling.' This means that if you command another to perform a marriage for someone else, that marriage is not attributed to you. But if you command another to marry you (i.e., as your proxy), the marriage is attributed to you.
The 'ruling' here is the legal effect of the marriage contract.
3
Apply the rule to the scenario of a young daughter
From al-Bada'i: If a father swears not to give his young daughter in marriage, and a man marries her without the father's order, but the father later consents, the father violates his oath. His right of consent is exercised through approval, so the marriage is attributed to him.
Consent (ijāz) after the fact makes the marriage his act.
4
Apply the rule to the scenario of an adult son
If a father swears not to give his adult son in marriage, and commands someone to marry the son, and then the son (who is adult) consents, the father does not violate the oath. The son's consent is separate, and the father's command does not attach the marriage to him.
For an adult, the father's role is only recommendatory; the son's independent consent matters.
5
Distinguish between consent by speech and by action
The passage ends with a reference: 'If he swore not to marry, and consented by speech, he violates; by action, he does not.' This further nuance shows that the form of consent matters for tazawwuj oaths.
This part is cited for completeness; full explanation is promised later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالنكاح لأنه لو قال والله لا أزوج فلانة فأمر رجلاً فزوجها لا يحنث بخلاف التزوج. قال محمد بن الوليد : سألت نجم الدين عن الفرق فقال : التزويج بأمره لا يلحقه حكمه والتزوج بأمره يثبت حكمه لو وهو الحل؛ كذا في الفيض معزياً إلى مجموع النوازل وفي البدائع : حلف لا يزوج بنته الصغيرة فتزوجها رجل بغير أمره فأجاز حنث لأن حقوقه تتعلق بالمجيز، ولو حلف لا يزوج ابناً له كبيراً فأمر رجلاً فزوجه ثم بلغ الابن فأجاز أو زوجه رجل وأجاز الأب ورضي الابن لم يحنث، وسيأتي تمامه في قوله لو حلف لا يتزوج فأجاز بالقول حنث وبالفعل لا».
And it is restricted by marriage because if he said 'By Allah, I will not give so-and-so in marriage,' then commanded a man and he married her [to someone], he does not violate, contrary to marrying [himself]. Muhammad ibn al-Walid said: I asked Najm al-Din about the difference, and he said: Tazwīj (giving in marriage) by his command does not attach its ruling to him, and tazawwuj (marrying) by his command establishes its ruling, even if it is [only] the permission; as in al-Fayd, attributed to Majmu' al-Nawazil. And in al-Bada'i: If he swore not to give his young daughter in marriage, then a man married her without his order, and he consented, he violates because his rights are attached to the consenter; if he swore not to give his adult son in marriage, then commanded a man and he married him, then the son reached puberty and consented, or a man married him and the father consented and the son was pleased, he does not violate. Its completion will come in his statement: 'If he swore not to marry, and consented by speech, he violates; by action, he does not.'
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of marriage-related oaths: 'tazwij' (giving someone in marriage, as a guardian) and 'tazawwuj' (marrying oneself). The key difference is that if a guardian swears not to give his ward in marriage, ordering another to perform the marriage does not violate the oath because the guardian's right is to consent or perform the contract, but the act of contracting is done by another. However, if a man swears not to marry himself (tazawwuj), ordering a proxy to contract the marriage does violate because the marriage is attributed to him. The text cites Najm al-Din's explanation: tazwīj by command does not attach its ruling (i.e., the marriage contract) to the commander, whereas tazawwuj by command does attach its ruling. Then it gives examples from al-Bada'i: for a young daughter, if someone else marries her without the father's permission and the father consents later, he violates his oath because his right of consent is exercised by the consent. For an adult son, if the father commands a proxy to marry him off, and the son later consents, the oath is not violated because the father's role ended with the command, and the son's consent is separate. The passage promises further details later regarding the effect of consent by speech vs. action.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwīj): The act of giving someone in marriage, i.e., performing the marriage contract for another person as a guardian or agent.
تزوج (tazawwuj): The act of marrying oneself, i.e., entering into a marriage contract for oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not give my daughter in marriage.' He then commands a friend to marry her to someone, and the friend does. Does the man violate his oath?
A) Yes, because he commanded it.
B) No, because tazwīj by command does not attach its ruling to him.
C) Yes, because marriage is a sensitive matter.
D) No, because the daughter is young and cannot contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between oaths of tazwīj and tazawwuj: commanding a proxy for tazwīj does not violate the oath, but for tazawwuj it does.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
15 min
Various Marriage Oath Scenarios
Collection of oath case rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the presented marriage oath scenarios and explain the reasoning based on existence, location, definiteness, and intent.

Core Idea
Oaths are interpreted literally, but legal stratagems (makhraj) and intent (niyyah) can affect the ruling.

This lesson presents a series of case studies from al-Zahiriyya and other authoritative sources on oaths related to marriage. Each case demonstrates how the Hanafi school applies principles of existence, location, definiteness, and intent to determine whether an oath is violated. Understanding these scenarios will sharpen your ability to apply fiqh rules to real-world oath situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key elements of each case
For each scenario, note the subject of the oath, the condition, and what actually occurred.
The text presents over a dozen distinct cases; take them one at a time.
2
Determine the ruling
Does the oath-maker violate or not? Look for the explicit ruling in the text.
The ruling is usually stated as 'yahinthu' (violates) or 'la yahinthu' (does not violate).
3
Analyze the reasoning
Find the legal basis: existence at time of oath, place of contract, definiteness via kaf al-khitab, or intent.
Some cases rely on the concept of 'consideration' (i'tibar) of time or place.
4
Note any madhhab differences
When Abu Hanifa differs from Muhammad, record both positions and the basis.
The Basra case shows Abu Hanifa's preference for birthplace; Muhammad's view on the woman with a prior husband is also noted.
5
Apply the concept of makhraj
Understand how legal stratagems (e.g., appointing agents outside the forbidden place) avoid violation.
The makhraj is permitted in Hanafi fiqh to prevent sin while upholding the literal wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : رجل قال لامرأة لا يحل له نكاحها إن تزوجتك فعبدي حر فتزوجها حنث لأن يمينه تنصرف إلى ما يتصور عبد حلف أن لا يتزوج فزوجه مولاه وهو كاره لذلك لم يحنث لأن لفظ النكاح وجد من المولى. ولو حلف رجل أن لا يتزوج امرأة فاكره على النكاح فتزوج حنث في يمينه لأنه وجد لفظ النكاح منه رجل حلف أن لا يتزوج من أهل هذه الدار وليس للدار أهل ثم سكنها قوم فتزوج منهم أو قال لا أتزوج من بنات فلان وليس لفلان بنت ثم ولدت له بنت فتزوجها الحالف لا يحنث ولو حلف لا يتزوج من أهل الكوفة فتزوج امرأة من أهل الكوفة لم تكن ولدت قبل اليمين حنث ولو حلف أن لا يتزوج بالكوفة ثم أراد أن يتزوج فالمخرج له أن يوكل الرجل وكيلاً والمرأة كذلك ثم يخرج الوكيلان ويعقدان عقد النكاح خارج الكوفة فلا يحنث الحالف لأن المعتبر مكان العقد. ولو حلف لا يتزوج امرأة إلا على أربعة دراهم فتزوج امرأة على أربعة دراهم وكمل القاضي عشرة أو زاد الزوج بعد العقد من تلقاء نفسه في مهرها لا يحنث ولو حلف لا يتزوج من نساء أهل البصرة فتزوج امرأة كانت ولدت بالبصرة ونشأت بالكوفة يحنث الحالف في قول أبي حنيفة لأن المعتبر عنده في هذا المولد دون المنشأ. ولو حلف لا يتزوج امرأة كان لها زوج قبله فطلق امرأته تطليقة بائنة ثم تزوجها قال محمد لا يحنث في يمينه لأن يمينه تنصرف إلى غيرها. ولو طلق امرأته ثم قال إن تزوجت امرأة باسمك فهي طالق ثم تزوجها لم تطلق. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة بهذا الاسم فهي طالق فتزوجها طلقت . والفرق أن فيما تقدم صارت معرفة بكاف الخطاب فلا تدخل تحت النكرة، وفيما تأخر لم تصر معرفة فتدخل تحت النكرة. ولو حلف لا يتزوج امرأة على وجه الأرض ونوى امرأة بعينها دين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لا في القضاء، ولو نوى كوفية أو بصرية لا يدين أصلاً، وكذا لو نوى امرأة عوراء أو عمياء، ولو نوى عربية أو حبشية دين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لأنه نوى الجنس اهـ .
In al-Zahiriya: A man said to a woman not lawful for him to marry, 'If I marry you, my slave is free,' then he married her – he violates because his oath is directed to what is conceivable. A slave swore that he would not marry, and his master married him off while he disliked that – he does not violate because the word of marriage came from the master. If a man swore that he would not marry a woman, then he was forced into marriage and married, he violates his oath because the word of marriage came from him. A man swore that he would not marry from the people of this house, and the house had no people, then people moved in and he married one of them – he does not violate. Or he said, 'I will not marry any of the daughters of so-and-so' and so-and-so had no daughter, then a daughter was born to him and he married her – he does not violate. If he swore not to marry from the people of Kufa, then married a woman from the people of Kufa who was not born before the oath, he violates. If he swore not to marry in Kufa, then wanted to marry, the way out is that he appoints a male agent and a female agent, then both agents go out and perform the marriage contract outside Kufa, so he does not violate because the consideration is the place of the contract. If he swore not to marry a woman except for four dirhams, then married a woman for four dirhams and the judge completed it to ten, or the husband increased the mahr after the contract on his own, he does not violate. If he swore not to marry from the women of the people of Basra, then married a woman who was born in Basra but raised in Kufa, he violates according to Abu Hanifa because the consideration with him is place of birth, not upbringing. If he swore not to marry a woman who had a husband before him, then he divorced his wife with a final divorce and then married her, Muhammad said he does not violate his oath because his oath refers to another. If he divorced his wife and then said, 'If I marry a woman with your name, she is divorced,' then he married her, she is not divorced. If he said, 'If I marry a woman with this name, she is divorced,' then he married her, she is divorced. The difference is that in the first case she became definite by the letter of address (kaf al-khitab), so she does not fall under the indefinite; in the latter she did not become definite, so she falls under the indefinite. If he swore not to marry a woman on the face of the earth and intended a specific woman, he is believed religiously between himself and Allah, but not judicially. If he intended a Kufan or Basran woman, he is not believed at all. Similarly if he intended a one-eyed or blind woman. If he intended an Arab or Abyssinian woman, he is believed religiously because he intended the genus. End.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage collects numerous case examples from al-Zahiriyya and other sources regarding oaths about marriage. The first case: a man makes a conditional oath regarding a woman not lawful for him (e.g., a mahram or already married), then marries her – he violates because the condition was possible (conceivable). A slave's marriage by his master does not violate the slave's oath because the contract is by the master. Forced marriage: if someone is coerced into marriage, he violates his oath because the utterance comes from him. Hypothetical cases: if the object of the oath (e.g., 'people of this house') does not exist at the time of oath, then later comes into existence and he marries, he does not violate because the oath referred to the existing category. The same for daughters. For oaths based on location: if he swears not to marry from Kufa, the criterion is birth; if the woman was born elsewhere but lives in Kufa, he does not violate. A trick (makhraj) for an oath not to marry in Kufa: appoint agents to contract outside Kufa. For an oath with a mahr condition: if the condition is met but the court or husband later increases the mahr, it does not violate the oath. The madhhab difference: Abu Hanifa considers birthplace, not upbringing, for determining 'people of Basra'. Muhammad says that if a man swears not to marry a woman who had a husband before him, then divorces his wife and marries her, it does not violate because the oath refers to a different woman (i.e., one who had a husband at the time of the oath, not after divorce). The final part discusses intent: if one intends a specific woman, it is valid religiously but not in court; if one intends a type (e.g., ethnic group), it is not even religiously valid because it contradicts the general wording. However, intending 'Arab' or 'Abyssinian' is considered a genus and thus religiously valid.

Key Terms
مخرج (makhraj): A legal stratagem to avoid violating an oath without lying.
كاف الخطاب (kāf al-khiṭāb): The letter kāf used for addressing the second person, which renders a noun definite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not marry from the people of this house' when the house is empty. Later, a family moves in, and he marries a woman from that house. According to the text, does he violate his oath?
A) Yes, because the house now has people.
B) No, because the oath referred to the people at the time of the oath, which did not exist.
C) Yes, because the oath was general.
D) No, because he can use a makhraj.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies specific rules of existence, location, definiteness, and intent to determine whether a marriage oath is violated; legal stratagems (makhraj) are permitted to avoid violation without lying.

Slave's Claim of Prayer in Conditional Manumission
Ruling on a slave's claim of praying to trigger conditional freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave claims performance of an observable act in a conditional manumission and explain why the master's denial prevails.

Core Idea
For observable acts, the master's denial outweighs the slave's bare claim.

This passage from Al-Muḥīṭ addresses a specific scenario in conditional manumission: a master says to his slave, 'If you pray, you are free.' The slave then claims he prayed, but the master denies. The ruling is clear: the slave is not freed. Why? Because prayer is an external, observable act that could have been witnessed by others. The slave's mere assertion is insufficient against the master's denial. Understanding this principle helps us grasp the Hanafi distinction between observable and private matters in claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The master stipulated a condition: 'If you pray, you are free.' This is a suspended (mu'allaq) manumission.
The condition is an act of worship: prayer.
2
Note the claim and denial
The slave claims he performed the act; the master denies it. There is no third-party witness.
3
Apply the principle of observable acts
The text states that prayer is among 'observable matters' (al-umūr al-ẓāhirah). Such matters can be verified by others without difficulty. Therefore, the claimant's bare word is not sufficient; evidence is needed.
Contrast with inner states (e.g., intention) where the claimant might be believed.
4
State the ruling
The slave is not freed because his claim, without corroboration, does not overcome the master's denial.
This is the established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال لعبده إن صليت فأنت فقال صليت وأنكر المولى لا يعتق لأنه الأمور الظاهرة يمكن لغيره الوقوف عليه بلا حرج اهـ .
And in Al-Muḥīṭ: If he said to his slave, 'If you pray, you are free,' and the slave said, 'I prayed,' but the master denied, he (the slave) is not freed, because external matters can be verified by others without difficulty.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This ruling addresses a conditional freedom: if the master says 'if you pray, you are free,' and the slave claims he prayed but the master denies, the slave is not freed. The reason: prayer is an external act that can be witnessed by others; it is not a private matter solely known to the slave. Since other people could have observed him, his mere claim is insufficient against the master's denial. In other matters that are inherently private (like intention), the slave might be believed. This rule applies to oaths and conditions generally: the burden of proof lies on the claimant for observable acts.

Key Terms
الأمور الظاهرة (al-umūr al-ẓāhirah): Matters that are external and observable by others, such as ritual prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the slave not freed despite claiming he prayed?
A) Because prayer cannot be done by a slave
B) Because prayer is an observable act and can be verified
C) Because the master's oath is invalid
D) Because the condition was unclear

Key Takeaway
In conditional manumission, if the condition is an observable act, the master's denial of the slave's claim prevents the manumission.
